Definition of Genus: Jins as a Universal Predicated of Many Differing in Reality
Author defines genus and its types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus in any definition and distinguish between proximate and remote genus.

Core Idea
Genus is the universal that answers 'what is it?' across different species.

In this lesson, we examine the definition of genus (jins), one of the five universals. The author lays out precise conditions: genus is a universal predicated of many individuals that differ in their essences, and it answers the question 'what is it?'. Understanding these conditions allows you to correctly use genus in definitions and to distinguish it from other universals like species, differentia, and property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
What is being defined? It is 'jins' (genus). The author gives a complete definition.
2
Extract the definition components
The definition has three parts: (1) it is a universal (kulli), (2) it is predicated of many individuals that differ in their realities, (3) it answers the question 'what is it?'. Each part distinguishes genus from other universals.
3
Understand the exclusion clauses
'Differing in realities' excludes species (whose members share the same reality), differentia (not predicated of many), and property (accident). 'In answer to what is it' excludes the remaining universals like differentia and accident.
4
Distinguish proximate vs remote genus
Check whether the genus answers about the essence and all participants (proximate) or only some (remote). Example: 'animal' for human is proximate, 'body' is remote.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكليات الخمس ( فَالْكُلِّيَّاتُ خَمْسٌ)، لا أزيد ولا أنقص. الْمَبْحَثُ الأَوَّلُ: الْجِنْسُ . الْأَوَّلُ: الْجِنْسُ ، وَهُوَ كُلِّي، مَقُولٌ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ مَوْجُودِينَ أَوْ مَعْدُومِينَ، (مُخْتَلِفِينَ بِالْحَقَائِقِ)، احْتَرَزَ بِهِ عَنِ النَّوْعِ وَفَصْلِهِ وَخَاصَّتِهِ، (فِي جَوَابِ مَا هُوَ)، احْتَرَزَ بِهِ عَنِ الْبَوَاقِي، (فَإِنْ كَانَ) الْجِنْسُ (جَوَابًا عَنِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ) الَّتِي هُوَ جِنْسٌ لَهَا، (وَ) (عَنْ جَمِيعِ الْمُشَارِكَاتِ فَقَرِيبٌ)، كَالْحَيَوَانِ، (وَإِلَّا) يَكُنْ جَوَابًا عَنِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ وَعَنْ جَمِيعِ الْمُشَارِكَاتِ، بَلْ عَنْهَا وَعَنْ بَعْضِهَا فَقَطْ (فَبَعِيدٌ)، كَالْجِسْمِ.
Chapter on the five universals: The universals are five, no more and no less. The first discussion: Genus. The first: Genus, and it is a universal predicated of many existing or non-existing, differing in realities—this excludes species, differentia, and property—in answer to 'what is it?'—this excludes the remaining. If the genus answers about the essence of which it is a genus and about all the participants, then it is proximate, like 'animal'; if it does not answer about the essence and all the participants, but only about it and some of them, then it is remote, like 'body'.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage defines genus (jins) as one of the five universals. The definition includes three key components: genus is a universal; it is predicated of many individuals (whether existent in the external world or only in the mind); and those individuals differ in their realities (i.e., they are distinct species). The phrase 'differing in realities' distinguishes genus from species (whose members share the same reality), differentia (which is not predicated of many), and property (which is an accident). The phrase 'in answer to “what is it?”' distinguishes genus from the other universals such as differentia and property, which answer different questions. The author then distinguishes two types of genus: proximate (qarib) and remote (ba'id). A proximate genus answers the 'what is it?' question about the essence itself and all its subordinate species; for example, 'animal' is a proximate genus for humans, horses, etc. A remote genus answers about the essence and only some of the participants; for example, 'body' is remote for humans because it includes both living and non-living things. This classification is foundational for constructing definitions (hadd) in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
جنس
قريب
بعيد
كلي

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a proximate genus for 'human'?
A) Body (jism)
B) Animal (hayawan)
C) Substance (jawhar)
D) Walking (mashi)

Key Takeaway
Genus is a universal that answers 'what is it?' and is divided into proximate and remote based on how many participants it covers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Why One Quiddity Cannot Have Two Genera in the Same Rank
Author argues against two genera for a single quiddity and addresses objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument against two genera for a single quiddity and evaluate the objections and refutations presented by the author.

Core Idea
Two genera in one rank for one quiddity leads either to two quiddities or circularity.

This passage tackles a key principle in the theory of genera. The author argues that a single quiddity cannot have two genera at the same level of generality (rank). He considers two objections and refutes them, revealing the logical impossibility. Understanding this principle helps grasp how definitions are structured in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Find the statement 'لا يمكن جنسين في مرتبة واحدة لماهية واحدة'. This is the thesis: it is impossible for one quiddity to have two genera in the same rank.
2
Extract the first objection
The first objection: two universals with partial overlap could be genera for a shared quiddity. Locate the objection starting with 'أما أولا فلأن من الجائز أن يكون كليان بينهما عموم من وجه...'
3
Extract the second objection
The second objection: the definition of genus as answering 'what is it' is merely conventional. Locate 'فلأن كون ما هو سؤالا عن تمام الماهية المشتركة اصطلاح'.
4
Reconstruct the refutation
The author refutes by presenting a conditional: if two genera were possible, either each is constituted by a differentia (giving two quiddities) or each is constituted by the other (circularity). Both are impossible, so the original thesis stands.
This is a conditional proof (qiyas istithna'i).
5
Apply the same reasoning to a fiqh example
Consider the quiddity 'contract' ('aqd). Could it have two genera like 'transfer of ownership' and 'obligation' in the same rank? Apply the same reasoning to see why not.
This demonstrates the principle's relevance beyond logic proper.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يمكن ومِنْ ههنا، أي من أجل أن الجنس جواب لـ«ما هو» وتمام مشترك جنسين في ( يَقْتَرحُ ) ، أي يظهر (عدم إِمْكَانِ جِنْسَيْنِ فِي مرتبة واحدة لماهية واحدة). وإلا الماهية واحدة لم يكن أحدهما تمام مشترك .

مرتبة واحدة (1)

وفيه نظر ، أما أولا فلأن من الجائز أن يكون كليان بينهما عموم من وجه، مجتمعين في ماهية واحدة، متفارقين في الماهيتين، ويكونان جنسين لهما ، وكل منهما تمام مشترك بين تلك الماهية والأخرى التي توجد فيها . وأما ثانيا : فلأن كون ما هو سؤالا عن تمام الماهية المشتركة اصطلاح، فلا يبنى عليه القواعد العلمية، والمشهور في الاستدلال أنه لو أمكن جنسان في مرتبة فإما أن يتقوم كل منهما بفصل، فيحصل من اجتماعه مع كل منهما ماهية ، فصارا جنسين لماهيتين، أو يتقوم كل منهما بالآخر، فلا بد من تحصل كل منهما قبل تحصل الآخر؛ لأن الشيء متى لم يتحصل في نفسه لم يحصل غیر ه، وهو دور محال. ومن ههنا اتضح عدم إمكان جنسين بينهما عموم من وجه ، فتأمل فيه ؛ فإن فيه كلاما ظاهرا .
It is impossible. And from here, i.e., because the genus answers 'what is it' and is the common term of two genera, it is proposed, i.e., the impossibility of two genera in one rank for one quiddity appears. Otherwise, the quiddity being one, neither would be a complete common term. One rank (1). And there is consideration in it. As for first, because it is possible that two universals between which there is generality-specification in some respect, coming together in one quiddity, differing in two quiddities, are both genera for them, and each is a complete common term between that quiddity and the other in which it is found. As for second, because 'what is it' being a question about the complete common quiddity is a convention, so scientific rules are not built upon it. And the well-known in reasoning is that if two genera were possible in a rank, then either each is constituted by a differentia, so from its combination with each a quiddity results, and they become two genera for two quiddities; or each is constituted by the other, so each must be realized before the realization of the other, because a thing when not realized in itself does not realize other than it, and that is impossible circularity. From here it becomes clear the impossibility of two genera between which there is generality-specification in some respect, so ponder it; for there is apparent discourse in it.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument against the possibility of two genera in one rank for a single quiddity. The author begins by stating the impossibility, linking it to the definition of genus as answering 'what is it?' for a complete common term. He then considers objections: first, that two universals with partial overlap could be genera for a shared quiddity; second, that the definition of 'what is it' is merely conventional. The author refutes these by presenting the standard argument: if two genera were possible, then either each is completed by a differentia (yielding two quiddities) or each constitutes the other (creating circularity). Both options are impossible. The conclusion is that even universals with partial overlap cannot serve as two genera for one quiddity.

تمام مشترك
مرتبة
فصل
دور

Checkpoint

A) The quiddity becomes more specific.
B) Either two quiddities result or circularity occurs.
C) The two genera must be identical.
D) The quiddity is undefined.

Key Takeaway
One quiddity cannot have two genera in the same rank; any attempt leads either to two distinct quiddities or to logical circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Genus Requires a Differentia to Become Species
Author defines genus as unstable quiddity needing differentia for actualization

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a genus cannot be actualized without a differentia and identify the key components of this process.

Core Idea
Genus is unstable; only union with a differentia makes it a determinate species.

This passage from the chapter on the Five Universals tackles a foundational question: why does a genus need a differentia? The author argues that the genus is an unstable quiddity that cannot achieve actualization on its own. By understanding this necessary relationship, you will grasp how definitions are built in Islamic logic and how concepts like 'animal' become 'human'.

Guided Path
1
Understand the instability of the genus
The author states that the genus (jins) is an unstable quiddity (māhiyyah mutazalzilah) – it has no actualization before the species. Recognize that this instability means the genus alone does not give a determinate concept.
The genus is like a common feature shared by multiple species; it lacks specificity.
2
Recognize the need for an actualizer
The genus requires an actualizer (muḥaṣṣil) that removes its instability and unites with it, producing an actualized species. This actualizer is the differentia (faṣl).
The differentia provides the specific determination the genus lacks.
3
Examine the example of color
Consider the genus 'color' (lawn). When you think of color alone, it is ambiguous – it could be white (qābiḍ al-baṣar) or black (mufarriq). The mind is not satisfied until an additional meaning like whiteness or blackness is added, making color determinate.
This example illustrates how the differentia actualizes the genus.
4
Follow the metaphysical proof
The author provides a proof: the intellect considers quiddities in themselves and finds them equally balanced between determination and non-determination. The Agent (God) is generous and bestows existence. But existence cannot attach without union, so the quiddity must unite with the differentia.
This argument shows the necessity of the differentia from a metaphysical perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكليات الخمس. وَخَارِجًا، فَهُوَ مَحْمُولٌ عَلَيْهِ فِيهِمَا ) ، لا كما ظن أصحاب الانضمام. (وَمَنْشَأُ ذَلِكَ أَنَّ الْجِنْسَ) ماهية متزلزلة ، (لَيْسَ لَهُ تَحْصِيلٌ قَبْلَ النّوع ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ قَبْلِيَّةً لَا بِالزَّمَانِ) ، فلا بد من محصل يحصله، رافعا تزلزله، ومتحدا معه، فيصير نوعا محصلا، وقد عرفت تفصيله؛ (فَإِنَّ اللَّوْنَ مثلا إذا أَخْطَرْنَاهُ بِالْبَالِ فَلاَ يَقْنَعُ بِتَحَصُّل شَيْءٍ مُتَقَرِّرِ بِالْفِعْلِ)؛ لتزلزله في الوجود أنه بعينه قابض البصر أو مفرقه ، فكيف تقنع، (بَلْ يَطْلُبُ فِي مَعْنَى اللَّوْنِ بين زِيَادَة ، بأن يصير اللون محصلا معها ؛ حَتَّى يَتَقَرَّرَ اللون بِتَقَرُّرِهِ ويرتفع تزلزله (بالفعل). وبرهان تحقق هذه الماهيات أن العقل إذا لاحظها من حيث هي وجد نسبة التقرر واللاتقرر إليها متساوية، والجاعل جواد، فلا بد أن يفيض عليها الوجود، وإذ الوجود من غير الاتحاد ممتنع عليها، فلا بد أن تتحد بالفصل، وهذا لا يسكت المناظر ، بل لا يكاد يتم.
Chapter on the Five Universals. '...and externally, it is predicated of it in both' — not as the partisans of aggregation supposed. The origin of that is that the genus is an unstable quiddity, having no actualization before the species, even if its priority is not temporal. Thus it must have an actualizer that actualizes it, removing its instability, uniting with it, so it becomes an actualized species — and you have learned its detail. For example: if we call 'color' to mind, it is not satisfied with the actualization of some thing actually determinate, because of its instability in existence—whether it is precisely the seizer of sight or its separator—so how can you be satisfied? Rather, it seeks in the meaning of color an additional meaning, such that color becomes actualized with it, until color becomes determinate by its determination and its instability is actually removed. The proof of the realization of these quiddities is: when the intellect considers them in themselves, it finds the relation of determination and non-determination equally balanced. The Agent is generous, so He must bestow existence upon them. Since existence without union is impossible for them, they must unite with the differentia. This does not silence the disputant, nor does it nearly conclude.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition explaining why genus requires a differentia to become a species, refuting the view of the 'partisans of aggregation' (asḥāb al-inḍimām). The author first states that the genus (jins) is an unstable quiddity (māhiyyah mutazalzilah) and cannot achieve actualization (taḥṣīl) before the species, even if its conceptual priority is not temporal. Hence it needs an actualizer (muḥaṣṣil) that removes its instability and unites with it, producing an actualized species. The example of color (lawn) illustrates: color in itself is ambiguous—it does not satisfy the mind as a determinate actual thing because it could be either 'seizer of sight' (qābiḍ al-baṣar, i.e., white) or 'separator' (mufarriq, i.e., black). To become determinate, color must acquire an additional meaning (ziyādah) with which it unites, such as whiteness or blackness. This union resolves the instability. The author then provides a metaphysical proof (burhān): when the intellect considers these quiddities (the genus and differentia) in themselves, they are equally balanced with respect to determination and non-determination. The Agent (al-jā‘il), being generous (jawād), must bestow existence upon them. Since existence cannot attach to them without union, they must unite with the differentia. This argument is not easily dismissed by a disputant (munāẓir). In Islamic logic, the genus is like 'animal' which needs 'rational' to become 'human'.

Key Terms
الجنس
الفصل
تزلزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is temporally prior to the species
B) Because it is unstable and needs something to actualize it
C) Because it is composed of matter and form
D) Because it is a universal predicated of particulars

Key Takeaway
A genus is inherently unstable and cannot be actualized without uniting with a differentia, which provides the determination needed for a complete species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

5 min
Species nature does not require a differentia
Author explains that species, unlike genus, does not need a differentia to complete its meaning, only specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the species does not require a differentia and contrast it with the genus in terms of the two ambiguities.

Core Idea
Species is already determinate in essence; it only needs individuation, not a differentia.

In this passage, the author clarifies a key distinction between genus and species. While genus suffers from two types of ambiguity — essence and haecceity — species only has the latter. Understanding this helps you grasp why definitions need differentiae for genera but not for species themselves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two ambiguities of the genus
The author states the genus has two ambiguities: ambiguity of essence (ibhām al-ḥaqīqah) and ambiguity of haecceity (ibhām al-hīdhīyah). The first requires a differentia to specify the essence; the second requires a specifying agent.
2
Understand why species has only one ambiguity
The species is already constituted by genus and differentia, so its essence is determinate. Therefore, it only suffers from ambiguity of haecceity — which individual it refers to.
For example, 'human' as a species is clear in meaning; only which human is ambiguous.
3
See why species needs only a specifying agent
Since species lacks only haecceity, it does not need a constitutive differentia. It simply requires an external agent (like Allah) to bring it into existence and individuate it.
This contrasts with genus, which needs a differentia to become a species.
4
Apply to a real example
Consider 'animal' (genus) and 'human' (species). 'Animal' needs 'rational' (differentia) to become 'human'. Once 'human' is defined, it only needs an agent to create individual humans.
This distinction is crucial in ontology and definition theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا طَبِيعَةُ النَّوْعِ فَلَيْسَ يَطْلُبُ فِيهَا تَحْصِيلَ مَعْنَاهَا)؛ لعدم تزلزلها في قوامها، بَلْ إِنَّمَا يَطْلُبُ تَحْصِيلَ الْإِشَارَةِ) ؛ لإيهامها، ويتحصل باعتبار وجوده الخاص ، من غير زيادة أمر ، خارجا كان أو داخلا . واعلم أن في الجنس إبهامين إبهام الحقيقة وإبهام الهذية، وبالنظر : إلى الإبهام الأول يحتاج إلى الفصل ويتحد معه ليتحصل نوعا متحصلا، وبالنظر إلى الإبهام الثاني لا يحتاج إلا إلى الفاعل المشخص. وأما في النوع فإبهام الهذية فقط ، فلهذا لا يحتاج إلى أمر مقوم إلا إلى الفاعل ليوجده وليشخصه، وسيجيئ إن شاء الله تعالى تفصيل هذا المقام.
As for the nature of the species, it does not require the actualization of its meaning, because there is no instability in its constitution. Rather, it only requires the actualization of indication, because of its ambiguity; and it becomes actualized by considering its particular existence, without any additional thing, whether external or internal. And know: for the genus there are two ambiguities: ambiguity of essence and ambiguity of haecceity. With respect to the first ambiguity, it needs the differentia and unites with it to become an actualized species. With respect to the second ambiguity, it needs only the specifying agent. As for the species, there is only the ambiguity of haecceity; therefore it does not need any constitutive thing except the agent to bring it into existence and to individuate it. This matter will be detailed later, God willing.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage contrasts the nature of the species (tabī‘at al-naw‘) with that of the genus. The species does not require a differentia to complete its meaning because it is not unstable in its constitution (qiwām)—it is already a determinate essence. Instead, it only needs 'actualization of indication' (taḥṣīl al-ishārah), meaning it needs to be pointed out or individuated, because there is ambiguity in its reference (e.g., 'human' as a universal can refer to many individuals). This is achieved by considering its particular existence (wujūd khāṣṣ) without adding any constitutive element. The author then distinguishes two types of ambiguity (ibhām) in the genus: (1) ambiguity of essence (ibhām al-ḥaqīqah) — the genus is indeterminate in what it is, requiring a differentia to specify the essence; (2) ambiguity of haecceity (ibhām al-hīdhīyah) — the genus is indeterminate in terms of being this particular individual, requiring a specifying agent (fā‘il mushaṣṣiṣ). For the species, only the second ambiguity exists; hence it needs no constitutive differentia but only an agent to bring it into existence and individuate it. For example, 'animal' as a genus needs 'rational' to become 'human', but 'human' once defined only needs an external agent (Allah) to create individual humans.

Key Terms
النوع
إبهام الهذية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the species not require a differentia?
A) Because it is composed of matter and form
B) Because it has no instability in its constitution
C) Because it is a universal concept
D) Because it is created by the agent

Key Takeaway
The species is already complete in meaning; only its reference to individuals needs specification, unlike the genus which requires a differentia to complete its essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Distinguishing Genus, Species, and Matter via Three Conditions
Author distinguishes genus, species, and matter using three conditions on the same thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the three conditions (bi-shart 'adam al-ziyada, bi-shart al-ziyada, la bi-shart shay') to distinguish genus, matter, and species in any composite quiddity.

Core Idea
The same thing can be genus, matter, and species under different conditions.

In logic, we often encounter the terms genus, species, and matter. But the same thing, like body, can be called genus for one thing and matter for another. How is that possible? This passage resolves the puzzle by introducing three different ways of considering a thing with respect to added constitutive meanings. Understanding these conditions is essential for precise definition in Islamic sciences, especially in Kalam and Fiqh where correct categorization affects rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the example and the problem
The author uses 'body' (jism) as an example. Body is said to be genus for man (since it is predicated of man) and also matter for man (since it is a constituent part not predicated). Notice the apparent contradiction: how can the same thing be both genus and matter?
2
Understand the three conditions
The author introduces three ways of considering a thing with respect to additions (al-ziyada): (1) with the condition of no addition, (2) with the condition of addition, (3) without any condition. These are not changes in the thing itself but shifts in how we mentally abstract it.
These conditions are fundamental to the logic of definition in the Islamic tradition.
3
Apply each condition to body
Apply each condition: (a) Body taken with condition of no addition (abstracted from all specific forms) is matter—it is the substrate, a part and cause of the composite, not predicated. (b) Body taken with condition of addition (including constitutive meanings like animality and rationality) is the species (e.g., human). (c) Body taken without any condition (neither with nor without addition) is genus—it is predicated of every composite of matter and form, and remains unknown in terms of specific form.
Even if body in reality has a thousand constitutive meanings, when considered without condition it is still genus.
4
Generalize the principle
The author states that this ruling is general: it applies to things composite in essence (like body) and things simple in essence (like color). For example, color can be considered genus (without condition), species (with condition of addition, e.g., whiteness), or matter (with condition of no addition, as the substrate for specific colors).
This shows the logical tool is not limited to physical substances but covers all quiddities.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَا الْفَرْقُ بَيْنَ الْجِنْسِ وَالْمَادَّةِ فَإِنَّهُ يُقَالُ لِلْجِسْمِ مَثَلًا إِنَّهُ جِنْسٌ لِلْإِنْسَانِ فَهُوَ مَحْمُولٌ عَلَيْهِ لِكَوْنِهِ مِنَ الْأَجْزَاءِ الْمَحْمُولَةِ وَأَنَّهُ مَادَّةٌ لَهُ فَهُوَ مُسْتَحِيلُ الْحَمْلِ عَلَيْهِ لِكَوْنِهِ جُزْءًا خَارِجِيًّا. فَتَقُولُ: الْجِسْمُ الْمَأْخُوذُ بِشَرْطِ عَدَمِ الزِّيَادَةِ بِأَنْ تَكُونَ دَاخِلَةً وَمُتَّحِدَةً مَعَهُ مَادَّةٌ بِالْقِيَاسِ إِلَى الْمُرَكَّبِ وَجُزْءٌ وَعِلَّةٌ لَهُ وَإِنْ كَانَ نَوْعًا بِالْقِيَاسِ إِلَى مَا تَحْتَهُ مِنَ الْأَفْرَادِ وَالْمَأْخُوذُ بِشَرْطِ الزِّيَادَةِ بِأَنْ تَكُونَ دَاخِلَةً فِيهِ مُتَّحِدَةً نَوْعٌ وَالْمَأْخُوذُ لَا بِشَرْطِ شَيْءٍ مِنَ الزِّيَادَةِ وَعَدَمِهَا بَلْ كَيْفَ مَا كَانَ مُتَّحِدًا مَعَهَا أَوْ لَا وَلَوْ كَانَ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ مَعَ أَلْفِ مَعْنًى مُقَوِّمٍ فَهُوَ جِنْسٌ مَجْهُولٌ بَعْدُ لَا يُدْرَى أَنَّهُ عَلَى أَيِّ صُورَةٍ مِنَ الْعَنَاصِرِ وَالْأَفْلَاكِ وَمَحْمُولٌ عَلَى كُلِّ مُجْتَمِعٍ مِنْ مَادَّةٍ وَصُورَةٍ وَاحِدَةً كَانَتْ أَوْ أَلْفًا وَهَذَا الْحُكْمُ مِنْ كَوْنِ شَيْءٍ جِنْسًا بِاعْتِبَارٍ وَمَادَّةً بِاعْتِبَارٍ عَامٌّ فِيمَا ذَاتُهُ مِنْ مُرَكَّبَةٍ خَارِجًا كَالْجِسْمِ وَفِيمَا ذَاتُهُ بَسِيطَةٌ فِيهِ كَاللَّوْنِ.
What is the difference between genus and matter? For it is said of body, for example, that it is genus for man, so it is predicated of him because it is among the predicated parts; and that it is matter for him, so it is impossible to predicate it of him because it is an external part. So you say: body taken with the condition of no addition—such that the addition is internal and united with it—is matter relative to the composite, and a part and cause for it, even if it is species relative to what is under it of individuals. And that taken with the condition of addition—such that the addition is internal and united—is species. And that taken without any condition of addition or its absence, rather however it is, united with it or not, even if in reality there were a thousand constitutive meanings, is genus, still unknown, not known upon which form among the elements and spheres, and predicated of every composite of matter and form, whether one or a thousand. And this ruling—of a thing being genus in one respect and matter in another—is general in what its essence is composite externally, like body, and in what its essence is simple in it, like color.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a definitional explanation that resolves the apparent contradiction between the logical genus and the material matter of a composite substance. The author begins by posing the question: what is the difference between genus and matter, given that the same thing, e.g., body, is called the genus of man (since it is predicated of man) and also called the matter of man (since it is a constituent part that is not predicated)? The answer lies in three different ways of considering that thing with respect to added meanings (al-ziyada).

First, if the thing is taken with the condition of no addition (bi-shart 'adam al-ziyada), meaning that no additional constitutive meaning is included in it, then it is matter. Here, body considered merely as indeterminate corporeity serves as the material substrate for the composite human, and it is a part and cause of the composite. Even if this same body, when considered relative to its own individuals (like this specific body), is a species, in relation to the composite it is matter.

Second, if taken with the condition of the addition (bi-shart al-ziyada), meaning that the additional meanings (like animality and rationality) are included and united with it, then it is species. The composite whole, including the additions, is the species (e.g., human).

Third, if taken without any condition (la bi-shart shay'), neither with the condition of addition nor its absence, then it is genus. This consideration abstracts from any specific determination; the thing is just considered as it is, even if in reality it has a thousand constitutive meanings. In this sense, body is genus for all bodily substances, predicated of any composite of matter and form, whether one form or many. It remains unknown (majhul) in that one does not know which specific form it has (e.g., elemental or celestial).

The author concludes that this principle is not limited to composites like body but also applies to simple things like color, which in its own essence is simple but can be considered as genus (e.g., color) or matter (e.g., the substrate for specific colors) according to these three conditions.

الْمَادَّةُ
النَّوْعُ
بِشَرْطِ عَدَمِ الزِّيَادَةِ
بِشَرْطِ الزِّيَادَةِ
لَا بِشَرْطِ شَيْءٍ

A) When taken with the condition of no addition (bi-shart 'adam al-ziyada)
B) When taken with the condition of addition (bi-shart al-ziyada)
C) When taken without any condition (la bi-shart shay')
D) When taken as a composite of matter and form

Key Takeaway
The same quiddity can be genus, matter, or species depending on whether we consider it without condition, with the condition of no addition, or with the condition of addition.

Distinguishing Genus from Matter via the Three Condition Modes
Author distinguishes genus/matter and differentia/form via three condition modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a term functions as genus or matter based on the three condition modes (bi-shart 'adam al-ziyada, bi-shart al-ziyada, la bi-shart).

Core Idea
Genus is predicated; matter is not — the difference lies in how we consider the term's relationship to the composite.

This passage delves into the subtle distinction between genus and matter, and between differentia and form, using the concept of part-taking. The author uses the example of 'body' to show how the same term can be genus in one respect and matter in another, depending on whether additional differentiating meanings are included or excluded. This analysis is crucial for understanding definitions in logic and ontology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being analyzed
Notice that the author uses 'body' (jism) as an example. The same term can function as genus or matter depending on how it is considered.
2
Understand the three condition modes
Three modes: (1) with condition of no addition (bi-shart 'adam al-ziyada) — matter; (2) with condition of addition (bi-shart al-ziyada) — species; (3) without condition (la bi-shart) — genus. The condition determines predicability.
These are also called the 'three considerations' of part-taking.
3
Apply the modes to the example
Verify: body taken la bi-shart is genus (predicated of man); body taken bi-shart 'adam al-ziyada is matter (not predicated, an external part). Body taken bi-shart al-ziyada becomes a species (e.g., 'living body' is a species).
4
Extend to simple entities
For simple entities like color, the same analysis applies but the matter is intellectual, not physical. The author warns against the error of thinking that external simplicity implies intellectual simplicity.
This extension shows the generality of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكليات الخمس صرح في إلهيات الشفاء في مواضع غير عديدة أن الألوان بسائط خارجا مركبات ذهنا من الأجناس والفصول وبمثله قال في الهيولى وعلى هذين المذهبين لا بد من بيان الفرق بين المادة والصورة وبين الجنس والفصل فأراد المصنف أن يشير إليه فقال ما الفرق بين الجنس والمادة فإنه يقال للجسم مثلا إنه جنس للإنسان فهو محمول عليه لكونه من الأجزاء المحمولة وأنه مادة له فهو مستحيل الحمل عليه لكونه جزء خارجيا فتقول الجسم المأخوذ بشرط عدم الزيادة بأن تكون داخلة ومتحدة معه مادة بالقياس إلى المركب وجزء وعلة له وإن كان نوعا بالقياس إلى ما تحته من الأفراد والمأخوذ بشرط الزيادة بأن يكون داخلة فيه متحدة نوع والمأخوذ لا بشرط شيء من الزيادة وعدمها بل كيف ما كان متحدا معها أو لا ولو كان في نفس الأمر مع ألف معنى مقوم فهو داخل في جملة تحصيل معناه متزلزلة بين أن يكون معنى آخر جنس مجهول بعد لا يدرى أنه على أي صورة من العناصر والأفلاك ومحمول على كل مجتمع من مادة وصورة واحدة كانت أو ألفا هذا الحكم من كون شيء جنسا باعتبار ومادة باعتبار عام فيما ذاتها من مركبة في الخارج كالجسم وفيما ذاتها بسيطة فيه كاللون لكن يكون مادته عقلية على ما صرح به الشيخ الرئيس لا كما ظن أصحاب التلازم من أن البسيط في الخارج بسيط عقلا فمن فهم من هذه العبارة التلازم فقد غفل لكن في المركب تحصيل معنى الجنس المبهم عسير دقيق وفي البسيط تنقيح المادة ووجدانها متعسر ومشكل جدا فإن الجنس إنما يحصل في المركب بإبهام المتعين الذي هو المادة والمادة إنما يحصل في البسيط بتعين المبهم الذي هو الجنس فإن إبهام المتعين وتعين المبهم أمر عظيم عسير عند العقل وهذا هو الفرق بين الفصل والصورة فهذا إن أخذ بشرط عدم الزيادة فيه واتحادها معه صورة للمركب وعلة وجزء له مستحيل الحمل عليه وإن كان نوعا بالنظر إلى أفراده كالصورة الجسمية وإن أخذ بشرط الزيادة فهو نوع وإن أخذ لا بشرط شيء فهو الفصل المحمول على الجنس وعلى المجتمع من كل مادة وصورة ومن ههنا تسمعهم يقولون إن الجنس مأخوذ من المادة والفصل مأخوذ من الصورة فقد ظهر بهذا أن الأجزاء الخارجية متحدة بالذات والأجزاء الذهنية مغايرة لها بالاعتبار فلا يرد ما قال ناصرو التفارق وجوبا مع لو كان لشيء واحد أجزاء عقلية وخارجية لزم تعدد الحقائق لشيء واحد وأيضا يسقط ما في شرح المواقف من لزوم تعدد الحدود لشيء واحد هذا تلخيص ما في الحاشية
Chapter on the five universals. He stated in the Metaphysics of the Healing in many places that colors are simple externally, composite in the mind from genera and differentiae, and similar was said about hyle. On these two views, it is necessary to explain the difference between matter and form and between genus and differentia, so the author intended to point to that and said: What is the difference between genus and matter? For it is said of body, for example, that it is genus for man, so it is predicated of him because it is among the predicated parts, and that it is matter for him, so it is impossible to predicate it of him because it is an external part. So you say: Body taken with the condition of no addition, that the expressions are included and united with it, is matter relative to the composite, and a part and cause for it, even if it is a species relative to its subordinate individuals. And taken with the condition of addition, that the expressions are included in it united, is a species. And taken without any condition of addition or its absence, but however it may be, united with them or not, even if in actuality with a thousand constitutive meanings, it is included in the sum of the attainment of its meaning, wavering between being another meaning, a genus unknown yet, not known upon which form it is among the elements and spheres, and predicated on every composite of matter and form, whether one or a thousand. This judgment of something being genus in one respect and matter in another is general for that whose essence is composite externally like body, and for that whose essence is simple in it like color, but its matter is intellectual, as the Chief Master stated, not as the adherents of concomitance thought, that the simple externally is simple intellectually. Whoever understood from this expression concomitance has been heedless. However, in the composite, attaining the meaning of the ambiguous genus is difficult and precise, and in the simple, refining matter and finding it is arduous and very problematic. For the genus is only obtained in the composite by making ambiguous the determinate which is matter, and matter is only obtained in the simple by making determinate the ambiguous which is the genus. For making ambiguous the determinate and making determinate the ambiguous is a great matter, difficult for the intellect. And this is the difference between differentia and form. So this, if taken with the condition of no addition in it and its union with it, is form for the composite, and a cause and part for it, impossible to predicate of it, even if it is a species with respect to its individuals like the corporeal form. And if taken with the condition of addition, it is a species. And if taken without any condition, it is the differentia predicated on the genus and on the composite of every matter and form. From here you hear them saying: The genus is taken from matter, and the differentia from form. So by this it has become apparent that external parts are identical in essence, and mental parts are different from them by consideration. So does not follow what the proponents of necessary separation said: if something had both mental and external parts, then multiple realities for one thing would follow. And also what is in Sharh al-Mawaqif of the necessity of multiple definitions for one thing falls away. This is a summary of what is in the Hashiya.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and comparative analysis of two pairs of concepts: genus vs. matter, and differentia vs. form. The author begins by stating that Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is) held that colors are simple in external reality but composite in the mind from genera and differentiae; similarly for hyle (primary matter). This background sets up the need to clarify the distinction between matter and form, and between genus and differentia, since they are often conflated. The key move is to consider how a term like 'body' is used: when we say 'body is the genus of man', we predicate it, but when we say 'body is the matter of man', we do not predicate it because matter is an external part. The author then explains the three considerations. First, 'taken with the condition of no addition' (bi-shart 'adam al-ziyada): if the differentiating expressions are included and united with the term, it is matter relative to the composite, even if it is a species relative to its own individuals. Second, 'taken with the condition of addition' (bi-shart al-ziyada): if the expressions are included but as added separate entities, it is a species (or type). Third, 'taken without any condition' (la bi-shart shay'): no condition of inclusion or exclusion is attached, and it functions as genus, predicated on all composites of matter and form. Importantly, this analysis applies to both composite essences like body and simple essences like color, but for simple essences the matter is intellectual. The author then warns against the error of thinking that because something is simple externally, it is necessarily simple intellectually—that would be the 'concomitance' mistake. He notes that in composites it is difficult to attain the ambiguous meaning of genus, and in simples it is difficult to pinpoint the matter. The same threefold distinction applies to differentia and form: taken with no addition = form; with addition = species; without condition = differentia predicated on genus. The conclusion is that external parts are identical in essence to the whole, while mental parts are distinct by consideration, thereby refuting the objection that a thing would have multiple realities or definitions if it had both mental and external parts.

D) When taken as a species relative to its individuals

The three condition modes (bi-shart 'adam al-ziyada, bi-shart al-ziyada, la bi-shart) are the key to distinguishing whether a term functions as matter, species, or genus.

Objections 1-2: Hyle and Genera in Definitions
First two objections regarding hyle and genera.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaws in treating hyle as genus and form as differentia, and in assuming different genera for elements and heavens.

Core Idea
If hyle is genus and form is differentia, the differentia becomes too general.

The first two objections challenge the standard matter-form framework by showing that applying it to elements and heavens leads to contradictions. The first objection shows that if hyle (prime matter) is taken as the genus and form as the differentia, then the differentia would be more general than the genus, which is impossible. The second objection argues that if the genera of elements and heavens differ in reality, then body cannot be a single unified essence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection's structure
The objection states that the hyle of elements differs from the hyle of heavens in essence, while their forms agree in essence. If hyle without condition is genus and form without condition is differentia, then the differentia (form) would be more general than the genus (hyle).
2
Test the condition
Consider the logical rule: differentia must be narrower than genus. If form is the same for both elements and heavens, but hyle differs, then the differentia (form) applies to both, while genus (hyle) applies only to one. Hence differentia is broader.
3
Identify the second objection
Essences differ by differentiae and by genera. If elements and heavens have different genera, then 'body' cannot be a single reality shared between them.
4
Evaluate the implications
These objections target the coherence of the definition of body as composed of matter and form. They reveal that the matter-form framework cannot consistently define body as a single essence when elements and heavens have different prime matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكليات الخمس الأولن ما أورده وحيد العصر فريد الدهر أبونا وأستاذنا نظام الملة والدين أفاض الله تعالى عليه أنوار رحمته وأسكنه في مقام خلته: أنهم قالوا هيولى العناصر مخالف لهيولى الأفلاك بالماهية، مع اتفاق صورها في الماهية، فهيولى العناصر إن أخذت لا بشرط يكون جنسا والصورة فصلا إن أخذت كذلك، فيلزم عموم الفصل من الجنس، وقد منعوا ذلك. الثاني أن الماهيات كما يختلف باختلاف الفصول كذلك يختلف باختلاف الأجناس، وجنس العناصر مخالف لجنس الأفلاك؛ لكونهما مأخوذين من مواد مختلفة الحقائق، فيلزم أن لا يكون الجسم حقيقة واحدة. الثالث: قد صرح الشيخ أن جنس الإنسان مأخوذ من بدنه، وفصله من نفسه وقد قالوا النفس تبقى بعد خراب البدن فيلزم بقاء الفصل بدون الجنس، فتأمل فيه. الرابع: مرتبة لا بشرط شيء فصل، فيلزم تركيب الفصل، وهو خلاف ما تقرر عندهم، والإشكالان الأخيران إنما نشآ من القول باجتماع التركيبين في الإنسان، والأول من القول بالاجتماع في الجسم، فلو انكسر سقطت من البين، هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Chapter on the Five Universals. First: what was presented by the unique of the age, the singular of the time, our father and master, the glory of the religion and the faith—may God Almighty shower upon him the lights of His mercy and house him in the station of His love—that they said: the hyle of the elements differs from the hyle of the heavens in essence, despite the agreement of their forms in essence. So the hyle of the elements, if taken without condition, becomes a genus, and the form becomes a differentia if taken likewise; then the differentia would be more general than the genus, which they have prohibited. Second: that essences differ by differences in differentiae as well as by differences in genera, and the genus of elements differs from the genus of heavens because they are taken from matters of different realities; thus it would follow that body is not a single reality. Third: the Master (Ibn Sina) stated that the genus of man is taken from his body, and his differentia from his soul; and they have said that the soul remains after the corruption of the body, so it would follow that the differentia remains without the genus—ponder that. Fourth: the level of 'not with condition' is a differentia, so the differentia would be composite, contrary to what is established with them. The last two objections arise from the claim of the combination of two compositions in man, and the first from the claim of combination in the body. If this were broken, they would all fall away. This is what is correct, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a critical presentation of four objections raised against the standard matter-form theory as applied to elements and animate beings. The first objection concerns the hyle (prime matter) of the elements vs. the heavens: although their forms are identical in essence, their hyle differ. If hyle is taken without condition as genus and form as differentia, then the differentia (form) would be more general than the genus (hyle), which is impossible because the differentia should be narrower. The second objection argues that essences differ not only by differentiae but also by genera; if the genus of elements and heavens differ in reality, then body cannot be a single unified essence. The third objection notes that according to the Master (Ibn Sina), man's genus (body) is taken from his body and his differentia (rational soul) from his soul; but the soul survives death, implying the differentia exists without the genus, which breaks the dependency of differentia on genus. The fourth objection points out that if 'not with condition' (la bi-shart) is considered a differentia, then the differentia itself becomes composite (since it has conditions), contradicting the doctrine that the differentia is simple. The author then notes that the last two objections stem from the claim that man has two compositions (body-soul and matter-form), while the first stems from the claim of composition in body itself. He suggests that if this foundational claim were refuted, all objections would collapse. The passage ends with a statement deferring to God's knowledge.

B) The differentia would be more general than the genus
C) The form is different
D) The genus is not a genus

Key Takeaway
The first two objections reveal that the matter-form framework cannot consistently define body as a single essence when elements and heavens have different prime matters.

Objections 3-4: Survival of Differentia and Composition
Last two objections about differentia and composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to critique the dependency of differentia on genus using the example of the soul's survival, and explain why the differentia cannot be composite.

Core Idea
If the soul is the differentia and survives the body, then the differentia exists without its genus.

The third and fourth objections target the application of matter-form theory to humans. Objection 3 notes that according to Ibn Sina, man's genus (body) perishes while his differentia (rational soul) survives, implying the differentia exists independently of its genus. Objection 4 argues that if 'not with condition' is a differentia, then the differentia becomes composite, contradicting its required simplicity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the third objection
Identify the components: genus of man = body, differentia = soul. The soul remains after body's destruction. Therefore, differentia exists without genus.
2
Recognize the violation
The differentia should depend on the genus for its existence; it cannot exist alone. This objection shows a contradiction in Ibn Sina's framework.
3
Understand the fourth objection
The level 'not with condition' (la bi-shart) is considered a differentia. But if it has conditions, it is composite, but differentia should be simple.
4
Analyze the source of the objections
The author notes these arise from the claim of two compositions in man. If that foundational claim were refuted, the objections would collapse.

Chapter on the Five Universals. First: what was presented by the unique of the age, the singular of the time, our father and master, the glory of the religion and the faith—may God Almighty shower upon him the lights of His mercy and house him in the station of His love—that they said: the hyle of the elements differs from the hyle of the heavens in essence, despite the agreement of their forms in essence. So the hyle of the elements, if taken without condition, becomes a genus, and the form becomes a differentia if taken likewise; then the differentia would be more general than the genus, which they have prohibited. Second: that essences differ by differences in differentiae as well as by differences in genera, and the genus of elements differs from the genus of heavens because they are taken from matters of different realities; thus it would follow that body is not a single reality. Third: the Master (Ibn Sina) stated that the genus of man is taken from his body, and his differentia from his soul; and they have said that the soul remains after the corruption of the body, so it would follow that the differentia remains without the genus—ponder that. Fourth: the level of 'not with condition' is a differentia, so the differentia would be composite, contrary to what is established with them. The last two objections arise from the claim of the combination of two compositions in man, and the first from the claim of combination in the body. If this were broken, they would all fall away. This is what is correct, and God knows best.

Commentary
This passage is a critical presentation of four objections raised against the standard matter-form theory as applied to elements and animate beings. The first objection concerns the hyle (prime matter) of the elements vs. the heavens: although their forms are identical in essence, their hyle differ. If hyle is taken without condition as genus and form as differentia, then the differentia (form) would be more general than the genus (hyle), which is impossible because the differentia should be narrower. The second objection argues that essences differ not only by differentiae but also by genera; if the genus of elements and heavens differ in reality, then body cannot be a single unified essence. The third objection notes that according to the Master (Ibn Sina), man's genus (body) is taken from his body and his differentia (rational soul) from his soul; but the soul survives death, implying the differentia exists without the genus, which breaks the dependency of differentia on genus. The fourth objection points out that if 'not with condition' (la bi-shart) is considered a differentia, then the differentia itself becomes composite (since it has conditions), contradicting the doctrine that the differentia is simple. The author then notes that the last two objections stem from the claim that man has two compositions (body-soul and matter-form), while the first stems from the claim of composition in body itself. He suggests that if this foundational claim were refuted, all objections would collapse. The passage ends with a statement deferring to God's knowledge.

The Universal as Genus of the Five Universals: A Paradox and its Solution
Objection that universal as genus creates self-contradiction, with start of solution using essential predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the logical paradox when the universal is treated as a genus of the five universals, and identify the distinction (bi-i'tibar al-dhat) that begins to resolve it.

Core Idea
Making the universal a genus of the five universals creates a self-referential paradox that only a refined notion of universality can resolve.

In late commentaries on logical works, a debate arises: can the universal (kulli) itself be considered a genus that includes the five universals (genus, species, differentia, property, accident)? The objection is that this leads to a contradiction: the universal would be both more general than genus (since genus is one of its species) and more specific than genus (since it is an individual of that same genus). The author presents this paradox and offers a solution in terms of essential predication (kulliyyat al-jins bi-i'tibar al-dhat). Although the text is truncated, we can grasp the crucial move.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection states: if the universal is the genus of the five universals, then the universal is more general than genus (because genus is one of its species) and more specific than genus (because the universal is an individual of the genus). Thus one thing would be both more general and more specific.
The objection uses the fact that genus is one of the five, so if universal is genus, then genus is a species of universal.
2
Understand the paradox
This is a logical paradox: nothing can be both more general and more specific relative to the same thing. It violates the hierarchy of the five universals.
The hierarchy normally has genus above species, etc. Here universal would be both above and below genus.
3
Locate the solution's beginning
The solution begins: 'the universality of the genus is in terms of essence (bi-i'tibar al-dhat)'. This means we must distinguish between the universal as a concept that applies to many (its extension) and the universal as considered in its essence (its intension).
The solution likely argues that the universal is not a genus in the usual sense of predication, but in some other sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّابِعُ: قَالُوا إِنَّ الْكُلِّيَّ جِنْسُ الْكُلِّيَّاتِ الْخَمْسَةِ، فَهُوَ أَعَمُّ وَأَخَصُّ مِنَ الْجِنْسِ مَعًا؛ لكونه فردا له، فيلزم كون شيء واحد أعم وأخص. وَحَلُّهُ: أَنَّ كُلَّيَّةَ الْجِنْسِ بِاعْتِبَارِ الذَّاتِ، أي مصداق حمل الكلي نفس ...
Fourth: They said that the universal is the genus of the five universals, so it is both more general and more specific than the genus, because it is an individual of it, so it follows that one thing is both more general and more specific. And its solution: that the universality of the genus is in terms of essence, i.e., the truth of predication of the universal itself...

This passage is a critical objection and partial solution. The objection runs: The universal (kulli) is said to be the genus of the five universals (genus, species, differentia, property, accident). But then the universal would be more general than genus (since genus is one of its species) and more specific than genus (since it is a species of something? Actually the text says 'because it is an individual of it' – meaning the universal is an instance of the genus? The logic is: if the universal is genus of the five, then genus (as a specific universal) is a species of that genus, making the universal more general; but the universal itself is also one of the five universals, so it is a species of itself? The exact reasoning is obscure due to truncation. The author's solution begins: that the universality of the genus is in terms of essence (dhat), i.e., the truth of predication of the universal itself... The solution likely points out that the term 'universal' is said in multiple ways: it can be a genus for the five only in a certain respect, not absolutely. The cut-off prevents a full explanation.

First Three Objections on the Five Universals
Author presents three objections regarding hyulā, mahiyyāt, and soul-body composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three classical objections against the five universals and explain how the commentator argues they collapse.

Core Idea
Objections to the five universals show that logical definitions can conflict with physical composition.

In this passage from a logical commentary, three objections are raised against the theory of the five universals, concerning hyulā and form, the unity of body, and the survival of the soul. The commentator adds a fourth objection and then shows how all objections rest on assumptions that can be invalidated, thereby defending the universals.

Guided Path
1
Extract the first objection
Identify the claim that if hyulā (prime matter) is taken unconditionally as genus and form as species, then the species (form) would be more general than the genus, which is forbidden.
The objection uses the logical rule that species cannot be more general than genus.
2
Extract the second objection
Identify the claim that quiddities differ by genera; the genus of elements and heavens differ, so body cannot be a single reality.
3
Extract the third objection
Identify the claim that for man, the genus is from the body and the species from the soul, yet the soul survives the body, so the species would remain without the genus.
4
Identify the commentator's fourth objection
The commentator adds that if the rank of 'unconditioned' is a species, then the species is composite, which is absurd.
5
Analyze the commentator's resolution
The commentator states that the last two objections arise from assuming two compositions in man, and the first from composition in body. If these assumptions are invalidated, the objections fall.

الأولن ما أورده وحيد العصر فريد الدهر أبونا وأستاذنا نظام الملة والدين أفاض الله تعالى عليه أنوار رحمته وأسكنه في مقام خلته: أنهم قالوا هیولى العناصر مخالف لهيولى الأفلاك بالماهية، مع اتفاق صورها في الماهية ، فهيولى العناصر إن أخذت لا بشرط يكون جنسا والصورة فصلا إن أخذت كذلك ، فيلزم عموم الفصل من الجنس، وقد منعوا ذلك .
الثاني أن الماهيات كما يختلف باختلاف الفصول كذلك يختلف باختلاف الأجناس، وجنس العناصر مخالف لجنس الأفلاك؛ لكونهما مأخوذين من مواد مختلفة الحقائق، فيلزم أن لا يكون الجسم حقيقة واحدة.
الثالث: قد صرح الشيخ أن جنس الإنسان مأخوذ من بدنه، وفصله من نفسه وقد قالوا النفس تبقى بعد خراب البدن فیلزم بقاء الفصل بدون الجنس، فتأمل فيه .

Chapter on the five universals. First: What the unique of the age, the one of the time, our father and teacher, the pillar of the religion and the faith (may Allah shower upon him the lights of His mercy and settle him in the station of His intimacy) mentioned: That they said the hyulā of the elements differs from the hyulā of the heavens in quiddity, despite their forms agreeing in quiddity. So if the hyulā of the elements is taken unconditionally, it becomes genus, and the form, taken likewise, becomes species. This leads to the species being more general than the genus, which they have prohibited. Second: Quiddities differ by different species and also by different genera. The genus of elements differs from the genus of heavens because they are taken from matters of different realities. This leads to body not being a single reality. Third: The Shaykh stated that the genus of man is taken from his body, and his species from his soul. They said the soul remains after the destruction of the body, so the species remains without the genus. Reflect on this. Fourth: The rank of being unconditioned is a species, so the species would be composite, contrary to what is established with them. The last two problems arise from the claim that there are two compositions in man, and the first from the claim that there is composition in body. If these are invalidated, they fall away. This is what is correct, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents three objections (and a fourth added by the commentator) against the standard theory of the five universals. The first objections concerns hyulā (prime matter) and form: if hyulā is taken unconditionally (la bi-shart) as genus and form as species, then the species (form) would be more general than the genus (hyulā), which is prohibited in logic. The second objection argues that quiddities vary by both genera and species; since the genus of elements and heavens differ, body cannot be a single reality. The third objection notes that for man, the genus is from the body and the species from the soul; since the soul survives the body, the species would remain without the genus, an absurdity. The commentator adds a fourth: if the rank of ‘unconditioned’ is a species, the species itself becomes composite. He then notes that the last two (third and fourth) arise from the idea of two compositions in man (body-soul and matter-form), while the first arises from composition in body. If this is resolved, the objections collapse. The commentator advises reflection.

Resolving the Paradox of the Universal as Genus
Author resolves paradox of universal as genus via dual considerations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and resolve the apparent contradiction that arises when the universal is considered both more general and more specific than genus, using the distinction between consideration of essence and consideration of accident.

Core Idea
The same thing can have opposite predicates if considered from different aspects.

In this passage, the author presents a powerful objection: if the universal is the genus of the five universals, then it is both more general and more specific than the genus—an apparent contradiction. The solution is a masterclass in the art of distinguishing considerations (iʿtibārāt). You will learn how a single entity can bear opposite descriptions without logical conflict, and why this principle is essential for sound reasoning in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claims that the universal is genus of the five universals, making it both more general and more specific than genus. Why would that be a problem?
The objector sees a logical contradiction: one thing cannot be both more general and more specific than another.
2
Understand the solution's key distinction
The solution distinguishes between 'consideration of essence' (iʿtibār al-dhāt) and 'consideration of accident' (iʿtibār al-ʿaraḍ). What does each consider?
Essence relates to the thing's essential nature; accident relates to accidental attributes.
3
Apply the distinction to the paradox
The universality of genus is in terms of essence (it is essential to genus); the genus-ness of universal is in terms of accident (a portion of genus accidentally applies to universal). Hence, the apparent conflict disappears.
The universal is more general in essence, but more specific in accidental attribution.
4
Extend the principle to the self-predication issue
The universal is said to be an individual of itself. How does the same distinction resolve that? The universal is itself in one consideration and its own individual in another.
No real self-contradiction because the considerations are different.
5
Recognize the broader significance
Why is this principle—that judgments differ by difference of considerations—so crucial? Without it, wisdom itself would be invalidated.
Many apparent contradictions in theology, jurisprudence, and logic are resolved by distinguishing aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّابِعُ: قَالُوا إِنَّ الْكُلِّيَّ جِنْسُ الكليات الْخَمْسَةِ، فَهُوَ أَعَمُ وَأَخَصُّ مِنَ الْجِنْسِ مَعا؛ لكونه فردا له، فيلزم كون شيء واحد أعم وأخص. وَحَلُّهُ: أَنَّ كُلَّيَّةَ الْجِنْسِ بِاعْتِبَارِ الذَّاتِ، أي مصداق حمل الكلي نفس ذات الجنس؛ لكونه ذاتيا له، وَجِنْسِيَّةَ الْكُلِّي بِاعْتِبَارِ الْعَرَضِ، أي باعتبار عروض حصة منه له، وَاعْتِبَارُ الذَّاتِ غَيْرُ اعْتِبَارِ الْعَرَضِ، فالأعمية والأخصية باعتبارين، فلا خلف. وَبِتَفَاوُتِ الاعْتِبَارَاتِ يَتَفَاوَتُ الْأَحْكَامُ، وَمِنْ هُنَا تَبَيَّنَ جَوَابُ مَا قِيلَ: إِنَّ الْكُلِّيَّ فَرْدٌ لِنَفْسِهِ، يصدق على نفسه وغيره من الكليات، والفرد مغاير لما هو فرد له، فَهُوَ غَيْرُهُ، فيكون مسلوبا عنه بالحمل الأولى، وَسَلْب الشَّيْءِ عَنْ نَفْسِهِ مُحَالٌ. وجه الدفع أن الكلي عينه باعتبار نفسه، وفرده باعتبار عروض مبدأ له، فلا محذور. نَعَمْ، يَلْزَمُ كَوْنُ حَقِيقَةِ الشَّيْءِ عَيْنًا لَهُ وَخَارِجًا عَنْهُ، لَكِنْ لَمَّا كَانَ بِاعْتِبَارَيْنِ فَلَا مَحْذُورَ. وَمِنْ ثَمَّ قِيلَ: لَوْلَا الِاعْتِبَارَاتُ لَبَطَلَتِ الْحِكْمَةُ.
Fourth: They said that the universal is the genus of the five universals. Therefore it is both more general and more specific than the genus simultaneously, because it is an individual of it. So it follows that one thing is both more general and more specific. Its solution: That the universality of genus is in consideration of essence, i.e., the truth of predicating the universal to the very essence of the genus, because it is essential to it; and the genus-ness of the universal is in consideration of accident, i.e., in consideration of a portion of it accidentally occurring to it. The consideration of essence is not the consideration of accident. So the generality and specificity are by two considerations, thus no conflict. And by difference of considerations, judgments differ. From this is clarified the answer to what was said: That the universal is an individual of itself, it is true of itself and other universals, but the individual is other than that of which it is an individual, so it is other than itself, so it would be denied of itself by first-order predication, but the denial of a thing of itself is impossible. The refutation: The universal is identical with itself in consideration of itself, and its individual in consideration of an accidental principle occurring to it, so no absurdity. Yes, it follows that the reality of a thing is both identical to it and outside of it, but since it is by two considerations, there is no absurdity. Hence it is said: Were it not for considerations, wisdom would be invalidated.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection from the matn of Sullam: the universal (kullī) is said to be the genus of the five universals, making it both more general and more specific than genus (since it is an individual of genus). This seems contradictory. The solution distinguishes two considerations: the universality of genus is considered with respect to essence (dhat) — i.e., the universal is predicated of the very essence of genus, because it is essential to genus. On the other hand, the genus-ness of the universal is considered with respect to accident (arad) — i.e., a particular portion of genus accidentally applies to the universal. Since these are two different considerations, there is no real conflict: the universal is more general than genus in terms of essence, but more specific than genus in terms of accidental attribution. The same principle resolves the related issue of the universal being an individual of itself: in one consideration it is itself, in another it is its own individual. This shows that distinctions of considerations (i‘tibārāt) are crucial in logic; without them, contradictions would arise and wisdom (hikma) would be invalidated.

Key Terms
كلي
اعتبار الذات
اعتبار العرض
حمل أولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) By considering universality in terms of essence and genus-ness in terms of accident.
C) By stating that the universal is a different type of predicate.
D) By rejecting the notion of genus altogether.

The resolution of the universal-as-genus paradox teaches that logical contradictions are often dissolved by distinguishing the respects or considerations under which attributes are predicated.

Fifth Objection: Existence and Individuation of Genus
Author presents objection and partial solution using dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dilemma facing the genus as both existent and predicable, and articulate the classical solution based on disregarding individuating accidents.

Core Idea
Genus exists but is individuated; predictability remains when accidents are disregarded.

In the study of the five universals, a critical problem arises: if the genus exists in reality, it must be a particular individual—yet it is supposed to be predicable of many. This lesson unpacks the objection and the standard logical solution, which distinguishes between existence and conceptual consideration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The objection says: if genus exists, it is individuated so can't be predicated of many; if it does not exist, it can't constitute existing particulars. Locate both conditions in the Arabic text.
The horn about non-existence is often overlooked by beginners.
2
Analyze the first horn's implicit assumption
Why does existence imply individuation? Because everything that exists in the external world is a particular. But does that necessarily prevent predictability? The objector assumes yes.
Notice the objector equates 'being predicable of many' with 'being universal'.
3
Understand the solution's move
The solution concedes existence entails individuation, but denies that this blocks predictability. The key is to disregard (قطع النظر) the individuating accident. When we consider genus purely as a concept, it remains common.
This distinction between the thing in itself and thing as considered by the mind is crucial.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Take 'animal' as genus for 'human' and 'horse'. Animal exists as individuated in each animal, but when we consider animalness in abstraction, it is still predicable of both.
This shows how the solution works in a familiar Islamic taxonomy.
5
Recognize the text cut-off
The Arabic ends at 'بقي' (remains). The standard completion is that the genus remains a common nature (tabīʿa mushṭarika) when accidents are set aside.
The commentary supplies the missing conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْخَامِسُ قِيلَ: الجنس إِنْ كَانَ مَوْجُودًا فَهُوَ مُتَشَخص؛ لأن الوجود بدون التشخص غير معقول، فَكَيْفَ مَقُولِيَّتُهُ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ، وَإِلَّا يكن موجودا كَيْفَ يَكُونُ مُقَوَّمًا لِلْجُزْئِيَّاتِ الْمَوْجُودَةِ ؛ لأن مقوم الموجود موجود ؟. وَحَلُّه: إن أريد في الشق الأول أَنَّ كُلُّ مَوْجُودٍ مَعْرُوضُ التَشَخُص، فذلك مُسَلَّمٌ، لكن لا نسلم عدم المقولية على كثيرين، وَذَلِكَ دَلِيلُ التَّقْسِيمِ وَالِاشْتِرَاكِ؛ فإنه إذا قطع النظر عن العارض بقي
Fifth: It is said: Genus, if it exists, is individuated, because existence without individuation is inconceivable. So how can it be predicated of many? But if it does not exist, how can it constitute existing particulars, since the constituent of an existing thing must exist? Its solution: If it is intended in the first branch that every existing thing is subject to individuation, then that is conceded. But we do not concede the lack of predictability of many. And that is evidence of division and sharing. For when one disregards the accident, what remains...
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection: if genus exists, it must be individuated (mushakhkhas), contradicting its predictability of many; if it does not exist, it cannot constitute existing particulars. The partial solution concedes that every existing thing is individuated, but denies that this prevents predictability of many. The answer suggests that when one disregards the individuating accident, the genus remains as a common nature that can be predicated of many. The text cuts off, but the argument is that genus, though existing and individuated in reality, can be considered in abstraction (bi-qat' al-nazar) as common. This is a classic solution in logic: the universal as a concept considered by the mind (ma‘qūl) is predicable of many, even though its instances are individuated in external existence.

Key Takeaway
The dilemma of genus existence and predictability is solved by distinguishing between the individuated external existent and the universal concept considered by the mind when accidents are disregarded.

Analyzing the Dilemma of Genus: Existence and Individuation
Author presents and solves the objection that if genus exists it is individuated, and if not it cannot constitute individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a dialectical dilemma about the existence of genus and evaluate the author's solution using the concepts of accident and substrate.

Core Idea
Genus is a conceptual commonality that remains when accidental individuation is set aside.

This passage presents a classic objection against the reality of genus: either it exists and is therefore individuated (so how can it be universal?), or it does not exist and cannot constitute existing individuals. The author's clever solution distinguishes between the inherent essence and the accidental property of individuation, showing that genus can be a constituent without itself being a particular existent. Let's walk through the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The objection forces a choice: either genus exists (then it must be individuated, contradicting universality) or it does not exist (then how can it be a constituent of existing things?). Note the exact phrasing in the Arabic: 'إن كان موجودا فهو متشخص ... وإلا يكن موجودا كيف يكون مقوّما'.
Recognize that the dilemma is a typical dialectical tool (ḥujja tamthīliyya).
2
Examine the author's response to the first horn
The author concedes that if 'exists' means 'has individuation', then every existent is individuated. But he denies that this prevents predication: when we mentally set aside the individuating accident, the common substrate (maʿrūḍ) remains, allowing the genus to be predicated of many. This is the 'evidence of division and commonality' (دليل التقسيم والاشتراك).
The key move is abstraction (qaṭʿ al-naẓar).
3
Examine the author's response to the second horn
The author denies that individuation is identical to the genus or a part of its essence. Instead, he argues that individuation is a 'non-existential' (ʿadamī) notion derived from the essence which is individuated by itself at various levels of existence. Thus the genus can be a constituent without itself being a physical existent.
This subtle move preserves the genus as a conceptual constituent.
4
Practice applying the distinction
Take another universal, like 'humanity'. Use the same reasoning: humanity exists conceptually and is predicated of many individuals. When you abstract from the accidental features of each person (e.g., height, color), the common humanity remains. This shows the logical structure found in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْخَامِسُ قِيلَ: الجنس إِنْ كَانَ مَوْجُودًا فَهُوَ مُتَشَخَّص؛ لأن الوجود بدون التشخص غير معقول، فَكَيْفَ مَقُولِيَّتُهُ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ، وَإِلَّا يكن موجودا كَيْفَ يَكُونُ مُقَوَّمًا لِلْجُزْئِيَّاتِ الْمَوْجُودَةِ؛ لأن مقوم الموجود موجود. وَحَلُّه: إن أريد في الشق الأول أَنَّ كُلَّ مَوْجُودٍ مَعْرُوضُ التَّشَخُص، فَذَلِكَ مُسَلَّمٌ، لَكِنْ لَا نُسَلِّمُ عَدَمَ الْمَقُولِيَّةِ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ، وَذَلِكَ دَلِيلُ التَّقْسِيمِ وَالِاشْتِرَاكِ؛ فإنه إذا قطع النظر عن العارض بقي المعروض مشتركا، وإن أريد أن التشخص عينه أو جزءه فممنوع، كَيْفَ وَدُخُولُ التَّشَخُص في كُلِّ مَوْجُودٍ مَمْنُوعٌ، بَلْ هُوَ أَمْرٌ عَدَمِيٌّ، يُنْتَزَعُ مِنَ الْمَاهِيَّةِ الْمُتَشَخِّصَةِ بِنَفْسِهَا فِي مَرَاتِبِ الْوُجُودِ.
The fifth [objection]: It was said: 'If the genus exists, then it is individuated, because existence without individuation is inconceivable; so how can it be predicated of many? And if it does not exist, how can it be a constituent of existing particulars, because the constituent of an existing thing is existent?' And its solution: If what is intended in the first horn is that every existent is characterized by individuation, then that is conceded, but we do not concede the lack of predication of many. And that is evidence of division and commonality; for when the gaze is cut off from the accident, the substrate remains common. And if what is intended is that individuation is identical to it or a part of it, then it is denied. How [can that be]? For the entry of individuation into every existent is denied; rather it is a non-existential matter, derived from the essence individuated by itself in the levels of existence.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage presents and resolves a fifth objection regarding genus (jins). The objection is a dilemma: If genus exists in reality, it must be individuated (since existence implies particularity), so how can it be predicated of many? If it does not exist, how can it constitute existing things (since a constituent must exist)? The author's solution: In the first case, concede that every existent has individuation, but deny that this prevents predication. When we abstract from the individuating accident (e.g., particularity), the common substrate remains, allowing predication of many. This is evidence of division (taqsim) and commonality (ishtirak). In the second case, if one means that individuation is identical to or a part of the genus, that is denied. Individuation is not an intrinsic existent; it is a non-existential (ʿadamī) notion derived from the essence which is individuated by itself at different levels of existence. Thus the genus can be a constituent without itself being physical existence. This shows the logical subtlety of handling metaphysical categories.

مقوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because when we ignore the accidental individuation, the underlying common nature remains.
C) Because the genus does not actually exist.
D) Because predication does not require existence.

Key Takeaway
Individuation is an accidental, non-existential notion; setting it aside reveals the common genus that allows predication and constitution.
