Species as the Second Universal: Core Definition
Author defines species (nawʿ) and discusses a subtlety about its application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of species (nawʿ) and identify its key conditions and the nuance regarding the notional individual.

Core Idea
Species is the universal predicated of many agreeing in essence in answer to 'What is it?'.

Species is the second of the five universals and the foundation for classification in Islamic logic. This lesson unpacks its precise definition and a subtlety about whether the species is a composite or a notional individual.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The author begins by naming the second investigation: 'al-nawʿ' (species). Find this term in the Arabic and note that it is being defined as the second universal.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The definition states: 'the second universal is the species, and it is the one predicated of many agreeing in reality in answer to 'What is it?''. The genus is 'universal' (kulli) and the differentia is 'predicated of many agreeing in reality in answer to 'What is it?''.
The condition 'agreeing in reality' distinguishes species from genus, which is predicated of many differing in reality.
3
Analyze the composite vs. notional individual nuance
The author asks: when we say 'every reality is a species relative to its shares', do we mean a universal that is internal (dākhil) with restriction external (problematic), or a notional individual (al-fard al-iʿtibārī) obtained by the intellect? The first makes the reality a part of a composite, so it's not a species; the second is correct because the intellect restricts the universal to the species without adding a real part.
This nuance resolves a potential contradiction: how can a simple reality be a species if it must be predicated of many? The answer is that we consider it as a notional individual.
4
Test the definition with an example
Take 'human' (insān) as a species. It is predicated of Zayd, Amr, etc., who all agree in humanity. The genus 'animal' is predicated of humans, horses, etc. The differentia 'rational' is not included in the definition of species but aids identification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْمَبْحَثُ الثَّانِي: النَّوْعُ. الْكُلِّي الثَّانِي: النَّوْعُ، وَهُوَ الْمَقُولُ عَلَى الْكَثِيرَةِ الْمُتَّفِقَةِ بِالْحَقِيقَةِ فِي جَوَابِ مَا هُوَ. وَكُلُّ حَقِيقَةٍ بِالنَّسْبَةِ إِلَى حِصَصِهَا نَوْعٌ، إِنْ كَانَ الْمُرَادُ مِنْهُ مَا هُوَ الْمُشْتَهِرُ بَيْنَ الْمُتَأَخِّرِينَ مِنَ الْكُلِّي مَعَ التَّقْيِيدِ بِأَنْ يَكُونَ دَاخِلًا وَالْقَيْدُ خَارِجًا فَنَوْعِيَّةُ الْحَقِيقَةِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَيْهِ غَيْرُ ظَاهِرٍ، كَيْفَ وَحِينَئِذٍ صَارَ الْحَقِيقَةُ جُزْءًا لِكَوْنِهَا عِبَارَةً عَنْهَا وَعَنْ غَيْرِهَا الَّذِي هُوَ التَّقْيِيدُ، وَإِنْ كَانَ الْمُرَادُ الْفَرْدَ الِاعْتِبَارِيَّ الَّذِي يُحَصِّلُهُ الْعَقْلُ بِالتَّقْيِيدِ اللُّحَاظِيِّ فَالنَّوْعِيَّةُ صَحِيحَةٌ قَطْعًا. وَقَدْ يُقَالُ عَلَى الْمَاهِيَّةِ الْمَقُولِ عَلَيْهَا وَعَلَى غَيْرِهَا الْجِنْسُ فِي جَوَابِ مَا هُوَ قَوْلًا أَوَّلِيًّا، خَرَجَ بِهِ الصِّنْفُ، وَالْأَوَّلُ الْحَقِيقِيُّ وَالثَّانِي الْإِضَافِيُّ.
The second investigation: The species. The second universal is the species, and it is the one predicated of many agreeing in reality in answer to 'What is it?'. And every reality with respect to its shares is a species. If what is intended by it is what is famous among the later [scholars] of the universal with the restriction that [the universal] be internal and the restriction external, then the specific nature of the reality with respect to it is not apparent. How? For at that point the reality became a part because it is an expression of it and of other than it which is the restriction. And if what is intended is the notional individual that the intellect obtains by notional restriction, then the specific nature is correct absolutely. And it may be said of the essence upon which the genus is predicated and upon other than it in answer to 'What is it?' as a primary statement; thereby the variety (ṣinf) is excluded. The first is the real [species] and the second the relative [species].
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage defines species (nawʿ), the second universal, and clarifies two senses. First, the core definition: species is predicated of many that agree in reality (ḥaqīqa) in answer to 'what is it?' (e.g., 'human' answers 'what is Zayd?'). The author then discusses a subtlety: every reality (ḥaqīqa) is a species relative to its individuals (ḥiṣaṣ). But do we mean by 'species' a universal that is internal (dākhil) and the restriction (qayd) is external? If so, then calling a simple reality a species is problematic because the reality would become a part of a composite. However, if by 'species' we mean the notional individual (al-fard al-iʿtibārī) produced by the intellect through notional restriction (e.g., 'human' restricted to a particular), then it is valid. Finally, the distinction between real species (al-ḥaqīqī) and relative species (al-iḍāfī) is introduced: real species is the primary genus-species hierarchy; relative species appears when a genus is predicated of a narrower group, e.g., 'animal' said of 'human' and 'horse'—here 'human' is a relative species of 'animal'.

Key Terms
نوع
كلي
حقيقة
فرد اعتباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Animal (ḥayawān) in relation to human and horse.
B) Human (insān) in relation to Zayd and Amr.
C) Rational (nāṭiq) as the differentia of human.
D) Walking (māshī) as an accident.

Key Takeaway
Species is the second universal, defined as predicated of many agreeing in essence, and its application requires careful mental consideration of how the species concept relates to reality.

Real vs. Relative Species and Exclusion of Sinf
Author distinguishes real and relative species, excludes sinf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real species (al-nawʿ al-ḥaqīqī) and relative species (al-nawʿ al-iḍāfī), and explain why sinf (variety) is not a species.

Core Idea
Real species is primary; relative species arises when a genus is predicated of a narrower group; sinf is excluded.

In addition to the standard definition, the author introduces a second sense of species: a relative species that appears when a genus is predicated of an essence and other things. This lesson clarifies the distinction and shows why the variety (ṣinf) does not qualify as a species.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second definition of species
The author states: 'And it may be said of the essence upon which the genus is predicated and upon other than it in answer to 'What is it?' as a primary statement'. This defines a second type of species: the essence that the genus is primarily predicated of, along with other things (other species under the same genus).
2
Distinguish real species from relative species
Real species (al-ḥaqīqī) is the species we learned in Lesson 1: it is predicated of many agreeing in essence. Relative species (al-iḍāfī) is the species considered in relation to a higher genus: it is what the genus is said of. For example, 'human' is a relative species under 'animal'.
3
Understand why sinf is excluded
The author says 'thereby the variety (ṣinf) is excluded'. Sinf is a subdivision that does not agree in essence but in accident (e.g., 'white human' is not a species because 'white' is an accident, not an essential attribute). Sinf fails the condition 'agreeing in reality'.
4
Apply the distinction to a logical hierarchy
Draw a simple Porphyrian tree: genus = animal, species = human, horse, etc. Human is both a real species (predicated of individuals) and a relative species (under animal). Sinf would be like 'Arab' under 'human' – but 'Arab' adds an accidental property, so it's not a species.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْمَبْحَثُ الثَّانِي: النَّوْعُ. الْكُلِّي الثَّانِي: النَّوْعُ، وَهُوَ الْمَقُولُ عَلَى الْكَثِيرَةِ الْمُتَّفِقَةِ بِالْحَقِيقَةِ فِي جَوَابِ مَا هُوَ. وَكُلُّ حَقِيقَةٍ بِالنَّسْبَةِ إِلَى حِصَصِهَا نَوْعٌ، إِنْ كَانَ الْمُرَادُ مِنْهُ مَا هُوَ الْمُشْتَهِرُ بَيْنَ الْمُتَأَخِّرِينَ مِنَ الْكُلِّي مَعَ التَّقْيِيدِ بِأَنْ يَكُونَ دَاخِلًا وَالْقَيْدُ خَارِجًا فَنَوْعِيَّةُ الْحَقِيقَةِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَيْهِ غَيْرُ ظَاهِرٍ، كَيْفَ وَحِينَئِذٍ صَارَ الْحَقِيقَةُ جُزْءًا لِكَوْنِهَا عِبَارَةً عَنْهَا وَعَنْ غَيْرِهَا الَّذِي هُوَ التَّقْيِيدُ، وَإِنْ كَانَ الْمُرَادُ الْفَرْدَ الِاعْتِبَارِيَّ الَّذِي يُحَصِّلُهُ الْعَقْلُ بِالتَّقْيِيدِ اللُّحَاظِيِّ فَالنَّوْعِيَّةُ صَحِيحَةٌ قَطْعًا. وَقَدْ يُقَالُ عَلَى الْمَاهِيَّةِ الْمَقُولِ عَلَيْهَا وَعَلَى غَيْرِهَا الْجِنْسُ فِي جَوَابِ مَا هُوَ قَوْلًا أَوَّلِيًّا، خَرَجَ بِهِ الصِّنْفُ، وَالْأَوَّلُ الْحَقِيقِيُّ وَالثَّانِي الْإِضَافِيُّ.
The second investigation: The species. The second universal is the species, and it is the one predicated of many agreeing in reality in answer to 'What is it?'. And every reality with respect to its shares is a species. If what is intended by it is what is famous among the later [scholars] of the universal with the restriction that [the universal] be internal and the restriction external, then the specific nature of the reality with respect to it is not apparent. How? For at that point the reality became a part because it is an expression of it and of other than it which is the restriction. And if what is intended is the notional individual that the intellect obtains by notional restriction, then the specific nature is correct absolutely. And it may be said of the essence upon which the genus is predicated and upon other than it in answer to 'What is it?' as a primary statement; thereby the variety (ṣinf) is excluded. The first is the real [species] and the second the relative [species].
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage defines species (nawʿ), the second universal, and clarifies two senses. First, the core definition: species is predicated of many that agree in reality (ḥaqīqa) in answer to 'what is it?' (e.g., 'human' answers 'what is Zayd?'). The author then discusses a subtlety: every reality (ḥaqīqa) is a species relative to its individuals (ḥiṣaṣ). But do we mean by 'species' a universal that is internal (dākhil) and the restriction (qayd) is external? If so, then calling a simple reality a species is problematic because the reality would become a part of a composite. However, if by 'species' we mean the notional individual (al-fard al-iʿtibārī) produced by the intellect through notional restriction (e.g., 'human' restricted to a particular), then it is valid. Finally, the distinction between real species (al-ḥaqīqī) and relative species (al-iḍāfī) is introduced: real species is the primary genus-species hierarchy; relative species appears when a genus is predicated of a narrower group, e.g., 'animal' said of 'human' and 'horse'—here 'human' is a relative species of 'animal'.

Key Terms

نوع إضافي
صنف
ماهية

B) Human (insān) in relation to Zayd and Amr.
C) Walking (māshī) in relation to Zayd.
D) White (abyaḍ) in relation to human.

Key Takeaway
Species has two senses: real (absolute) and relative (dependent on genus); sinf is excluded because it does not satisfy agreement in essence.

Definition of Species and Its Predication
Author defines species with the condition of agreement in reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of species from the passage and explain its condition of being predicated of many agreeing in reality.

Core Idea
Species is a universal predicated of multiple individuals that share the same essence.

In the five universals, species occupies the second position. The author defines it precisely as a universal that answers 'what is it?' for many things that agree in reality. Understanding this definition is crucial for distinguishing species from genus and subspecies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The passage defines 'al-naw'' (species). Locate the first sentence: 'الْمَبْحَثُ الثَّانِي: النَّوْعُ) (۲) الكلي (الثَّاني: النَّوْعُ، وَهُوَ الْمَقُولُ)...'
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'universal predicated of many' and the differentia is 'agreeing in reality in answer to 'what is it?''. Write them down.
3
Understand 'agreeing in reality'
This means the individuals share the same essence (haqiqa), not just accidental properties. It excludes things that only share a name or accidental features.
This condition is key to distinguishing species from accidental universals.
4
Note the nuance on reality being internal or external
The passage discusses two interpretations: if the common reality is internal to the species definition, then the reality becomes a part, complicating species-ness. But if we consider the notional individual formed by the mind, species-ness holds.
This nuance prepares for later controversies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْمَبْحَثُ الثَّانِي: النَّوْعُ) (۲) الكلي (الثَّاني: النَّوْعُ، وَهُوَ الْمَقُولُ)، المراد باللام الكلي (عَلَى) الكثيرة (الْمُتَّفِقَةِ بِالْحَقِيقَةِ (١) ، في جَوَابِ مَا هُوَ، وَكُلُّ حَقِيقَةِ (٢) بِالنَّسْبَةِ إِلى حِصَصِهَا نَوْعٌ [حَقِيقِيٌّ ](۳)، إن كان المراد منه ما هو المشتهر بين المتأخرين من الكلي مع التقييد بأن يكون داخلا والقيد خارجا فنوعية الحقيقة بالنسبة إليه غير ظاهر ، كيف وحينئذ صار الحقيقة جزء لكونها عبارة عنها وعن غيرها الذي هو التقييد ، وإن كان المراد الفرد الاعتباري الذي يحصله العقل بالتقييد اللحاظ ، فالنوعية صحيحة قطعا. (وَقَدْ يُقَالُ عَلَى الْمَاهِيَّةِ الْمَقُولِ عَلَيْهَا وَعَلَى غَيْرِهَا الْجِنْسُ، فِي جَوَابِ مَا هُوَ، قَوْلًا أَوَّلِيًّا، خرج به الصنف (٤)، (وَالْأَوَّلُ الْحَقِيقِيُّ وَالثَّانِي الْإِضَافِيُّ ، وَبَيْنَهُمَا عُمُومٌ مِنْ وَجْهِ ؛ لتصادقهما في الأنواع المركبة، وتفارق الأول من الثاني في الأنواع البسيطة، كالوجود، وتفارق الثاني من الأول في الأجناس المركبة، كالحيوان، هذا ما عليه المتأخرون . (وَقِيلَ) : بينهما عموم وخصوص (مُطْلَقًا) ، هذا ما عليه القدماء. قال في الصنف: ثم إضافة قيد قولا أوليا مما صنعه صاحب الشمسية، وشارحه القطب، ولكن السيد في حواشيه قال: هذا القيد ـ أي قيد «قولا أوليا» ـ وإن أخرج عن الحد أخرج النوع عنه أيضا بالقياس إلى الأجناس البعيدة، فيلزم أن لا يكون الإنسان نوعا للجسم النامي ولا للجسم ولا للجوهر، مع أنه يسمى نوع الأنواع؛ لكونه نوعا لكل واحد من الأنواع التي فوقه، وأيضا النوع لما كان مضايفا للجنس فإذا اعتبر في النوع القول الأولي فلا بد من اعتباره في الجنس أيضا ، وإلا لم يكن مضايفا له، فيلزم أن لا تكون الأجناس البعيدة أجناسا للماهية التي هي بعيدة بالقياس إليها، فالأولى أن يُترك قيد الأولية، ويُخْرَج الصنف بقيد آخر ، ويقال : النوع الإضافي كلي مقول في جواب ما هو ، يقال عليه وعلى غيره الجنس في جواب ما هو.
The second topic: Species. The second universal: species, and it is that which is predicated of many things agreeing in reality, in answer to 'what is it?'. And every reality in relation to its particular instances is a [real] species. If what is meant is that which is well-known among the later scholars—the universal with the restriction that the [common] reality is internal and the restriction is external—then the species-ness of the reality relative to it is not apparent, for then the reality becomes a part since it is an expression of it and of other than it which is the restriction. But if what is meant is the notional individual that the mind obtains through the consideration of restriction, then species-ness is certainly correct. And it is sometimes said of the essence that is predicated of itself and of other than itself, namely genus, in answer to 'what is it?'—primarily. This excludes the subspecies. The first is real species, and the second is relative species. Between them is general-and-specific in some respects: they agree in composite species, but the first differs from the second in simple species, like existence, and the second differs from the first in composite genera, like animal. This is the view of the later scholars. And it is said: between them is general-and-specific absolutely. This is the view of the earlier scholars. He said concerning subspecies: Then adding the condition 'primarily' is something done by the author of al-Shamsiyya and its commentator al-Qutb. But al-Sayyid in his glosses said: This condition—i.e., the condition 'primarily'—although it excludes the subspecies from the definition, it also excludes species with respect to distant genera, necessitating that man is not a species of growing body, nor of body, nor of substance, even though he is called the species of species because he is a species for each of the genera above him. Also, since species is correlative to genus, if the 'primarily' condition is considered for species, it must also be considered for genus, otherwise it would not be correlative to it. This necessitates that distant genera are not genera for the essence that is distant in relation to them. So it is better to leave the condition of primariness and exclude the subspecies by another condition, and it is said: Relative species is a universal predicated in answer to 'what is it?' and genus is predicated of it and of other than it in answer to 'what is it?'.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage defines the second universal—species (al-naw')—and distinguishes its two types: real species (al-naw' al-haqiqi) and relative species (al-naw' al-idafi). It also critiques the inclusion of the condition 'primarily' (qawlan awwaliyyan) in the definition of species. The author begins by stating that species is a universal predicated of many things that agree in reality (essence) in answer to 'what is it?'. Every reality (essence) is a species in relation to its particular instances. However, a nuance arises: if we take the view of later scholars who define species as a universal with the restriction that the common reality is internal and the restriction is external, then the reality itself becomes a part, making its species-ness unclear. But if we mean the notional individual formed by the mind through restriction, then species-ness holds. Next, the passage presents species in another sense: it is sometimes said of the essence that is predicated of itself and of other things, i.e., genus, but with the condition 'primarily'. This excludes the subspecies (al-sinf). The first type (real species) is predicated of its instances without needing a higher genus, while the second (relative species) is predicated via a higher genus. The author then explains the relationship between them: they overlap in composite species (like man—real species for animal, relative for body), diverge in simple species (like existence—real species but not relative since no genus above it), and diverge in composite genera (like animal—relative species for body but not real species). This is the later view. The earlier view holds that the relationship is absolute generality and specificity (real species is always more specific). The author then critiques the addition of 'primarily' to exclude subspecies: al-Sayyid al-Sharif in his glosses on al-Shamsiyya argues that this condition would also exclude species from being a species of distant genera (e.g., man would not be a species of animal, body, or substance), contradicting the term 'species of species'. Moreover, since species and genus are correlatives, if 'primarily' is required for species, it must also be required for genus, leading to the absurdity that distant genera are not genera. Therefore, it is better to omit 'primarily' and exclude subspecies by another condition. The preferred definition: relative species is a universal predicated in answer to 'what is it?', and genus is predicated of it and of other things in answer to 'what is it?'.

الحقيقة

Real Species vs. Relative Species
Author distinguishes real and relative species with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real species (naw' haqiqi) and relative species (naw' idafi) and give examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Real species is ultimate; relative species can also be a genus to something lower.

The passage introduces two types of species: haqiqi and idafi. This distinction is crucial for understanding the hierarchy of universals. Real species is the lowest in the chain; relative species is a species from above but a genus from below.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Real species: predicated of its instances without being predicated of a higher genus. Relative species: predicated of its instances but also a genus is predicated of it.
The passage: 'وَالْأَوَّلُ الْحَقِيقِيُّ وَالثَّانِي الْإِضَافِيُّ'
2
Examples from the passage
Real species: al-wujud (existence) is simple and has no genus above it. Relative species: al-hayawan (animal) is a species relative to body (since body is a genus of animal), but animal is a genus of man.
3
Understand the hierarchy
Relative species is not the final species; it can be a genus to something else. The lowest species is called the 'ultimate species' (naw' al-anwa').
Man is an example of ultimate species.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(وَقَدْ يُقَالُ عَلَى الْمَاهِيَّةِ الْمَقُولِ عَلَيْهَا وَعَلَى غَيْرِهَا الْجِنْسُ، فِي جَوَابِ مَا هُوَ، قَوْلًا أَوَّلِيًّا، خرج به الصنف (٤)، (وَالْأَوَّلُ الْحَقِيقِيُّ وَالثَّانِي الْإِضَافِيُّ ، وَبَيْنَهُمَا عُمُومٌ مِنْ وَجْهِ ؛ لتصادقهما في الأنواع المركبة، وتفارق الأول من الثاني في الأنواع البسيطة، كالوجود، وتفارق الثاني من الأول في الأجناس المركبة، كالحيوان، هذا ما عليه المتأخرون . Relationship Between Real and Relative Species
Author explains the relationship between real and relative species with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the relationship between real and relative species and apply it to examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Real and relative species overlap in composite species but diverge in simple species and composite genera.

The passage explains that real and relative species are not identical; they share a partial overlap. Understanding their relationship helps in correctly classifying universals, especially in composite and simple cases.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two types
Real species: ultimate, no genus above. Relative species: also a genus to something below.
2
Identify the three cases of overlap and difference
1) They agree in composite species (e.g., man: real species relative to animal, relative species relative to body). 2) Real species differs in simple species (e.g., existence: real species but not relative because no genus above). 3) Relative species differs in composite genera (e.g., animal: relative species relative to body but not real species because it has a species below).
3
Understand the two scholarly views
Later scholars: general-and-specific in some respects (partial overlap). Earlier scholars: absolute generality-specificity (real species is always more specific).
This affects how we classify universals in a hierarchy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْمَبْحَثُ الثَّانِي: النَّوْعُ) (۲) الكلي (الثَّاني: النَّوْعُ، وَهُوَ الْمَقُولُ)، المراد باللام الكلي (عَلَى) الكثيرة (الْمُتَّفِقَةِ بِالْحَقِيقَةِ (١) ، في جَوَابِ مَا هُوَ، وَكُلُّ حَقِيقَةِ (٢) بِالنَّسْبَةِ إِلى حِصَصِهَا نَوْعٌ [حَقِيقِيٌّ ](۳)، إن كان المراد منه ما هو المشتهر بين المتأخرين من الكلي مع التقييد بأن يكون داخلا والقيد خارجا فنوعية الحقيقة بالنسبة إليه غير ظاهر ، كيف وحينئذ صار الحقيقة جزء لكونها عبارة عنها وعن غيرها الذي هو التقييد ، وإن كان المراد الفرد الاعتباري الذي يحصله العقل بالتقييد اللحاظ ، فالنوعية صحيحة قطعا. (وَقَدْ يُقَالُ عَلَى الْمَاهِيَّةِ الْمَقُولِ عَلَيْهَا وَعَلَى غَيْرِهَا الْجِنْسُ، فِي جَوَابِ مَا هُوَ، قَوْلًا أَوَّلِيًّا، خرج به الصنف (٤)، (وَالْأَوَّلُ الْحَقِيقِيُّ وَالثَّانِي الْإِضَافِيُّ ، وَبَيْنَهُمَا عُمُومٌ مِنْ وَجْهِ ؛ لتصادقهما في الأنواع المركبة، وتفارق الأول من الثاني في الأنواع البسيطة، كالوجود، وتفارق الثاني من الأول في الأجناس المركبة، كالحيوان، هذا ما عليه المتأخرون . (وَقِيلَ) : بينهما عموم وخصوص (مُطْلَقًا) ، هذا ما عليه القدماء. قال في الصنف: ثم إضافة قيد قولا أوليا مما صنعه صاحب الشمسية، وشارحه القطب، ولكن السيد في حواشيه قال: هذا القيد ـ أي قيد «قولا أوليا» ـ وإن أخرج عن الحد أخرج النوع عنه أيضا بالقياس إلى الأجناس البعيدة، فيلزم أن لا يكون الإنسان نوعا للجسم النامي ولا للجسم ولا للجوهر، مع أنه يسمى نوع الأنواع؛ لكونه نوعا لكل واحد من الأنواع التي فوقه، وأيضا النوع لما كان مضايفا للجنس فإذا اعتبر في النوع القول الأولي فلا بد من اعتباره في الجنس أيضا ، وإلا لم يكن مضايفا له، فيلزم أن لا تكون الأجناس البعيدة أجناسا للماهية التي هي بعيدة بالقياس إليها، فالأولى أن يُترك قيد الأولية، ويُخْرَج الصنف بقيد آخر ، ويقال : النوع الإضافي كلي مقول في جواب ما هو ، يقال عليه وعلى غيره الجنس في جواب ما هو.
The second topic: Species. The second universal: species, and it is that which is predicated of many things agreeing in reality, in answer to 'what is it?'. And every reality in relation to its particular instances is a [real] species. If what is meant is that which is well-known among the later scholars—the universal with the restriction that the [common] reality is internal and the restriction is external—then the species-ness of the reality relative to it is not apparent, for then the reality becomes a part since it is an expression of it and of other than it which is the restriction. But if what is meant is the notional individual that the mind obtains through the consideration of restriction, then species-ness is certainly correct. And it is sometimes said of the essence that is predicated of itself and of other than itself, namely genus, in answer to 'what is it?'—primarily. This excludes the subspecies. The first is real species, and the second is relative species. Between them is general-and-specific in some respects: they agree in composite species, but the first differs from the second in simple species, like existence, and the second differs from the first in composite genera, like animal. This is the view of the later scholars. And it is said: between them is general-and-specific absolutely. This is the view of the earlier scholars. He said concerning subspecies: Then adding the condition 'primarily' is something done by the author of al-Shamsiyya and its commentator al-Qutb. But al-Sayyid in his glosses said: This condition—i.e., the condition 'primarily'—although it excludes the subspecies from the definition, it also excludes species with respect to distant genera, necessitating that man is not a species of growing body, nor of body, nor of substance, even though he is called the species of species because he is a species for each of the genera above him. Also, since species is correlative to genus, if the 'primarily' condition is considered for species, it must also be considered for genus, otherwise it would not be correlative to it. This necessitates that distant genera are not genera for the essence that is distant in relation to them. So it is better to leave the condition of primariness and exclude the subspecies by another condition, and it is said: Relative species is a universal predicated in answer to 'what is it?' and genus is predicated of it and of other than it in answer to 'what is it?'.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage defines the second universal—species (al-naw')—and distinguishes its two types: real species (al-naw' al-haqiqi) and relative species (al-naw' al-idafi). It also critiques the inclusion of the condition 'primarily' (qawlan awwaliyyan) in the definition of species. The author begins by stating that species is a universal predicated of many things that agree in reality (essence) in answer to 'what is it?'. Every reality (essence) is a species in relation to its particular instances. However, a nuance arises: if we take the view of later scholars who define species as a universal with the restriction that the common reality is internal and the restriction is external, then the reality itself becomes a part, making its species-ness unclear. But if we mean the notional individual formed by the mind through restriction, then species-ness holds. Next, the passage presents species in another sense: it is sometimes said of the essence that is predicated of itself and of other things, i.e., genus, but with the condition 'primarily'. This excludes the subspecies (al-sinf). The first type (real species) is predicated of its instances without needing a higher genus, while the second (relative species) is predicated via a higher genus. The author then explains the relationship between them: they overlap in composite species (like man—real species for animal, relative for body), diverge in simple species (like existence—real species but not relative since no genus above it), and diverge in composite genera (like animal—relative species for body but not real species). This is the later view. The earlier view holds that the relationship is absolute generality and specificity (real species is always more specific). The author then critiques the addition of 'primarily' to exclude subspecies: al-Sayyid al-Sharif in his glosses on al-Shamsiyya argues that this condition would also exclude species from being a species of distant genera (e.g., man would not be a species of animal, body, or substance), contradicting the term 'species of species'. Moreover, since species and genus are correlatives, if 'primarily' is required for species, it must also be required for genus, leading to the absurdity that distant genera are not genera. Therefore, it is better to omit 'primarily' and exclude subspecies by another condition. The preferred definition: relative species is a universal predicated in answer to 'what is it?', and genus is predicated of it and of other things in answer to 'what is it?'.

Key Terms
العام والخاص من وجه
العام والخاص مطلقا

D) They are completely different

Key Takeaway
Real and relative species overlap in composite species but diverge in simple species and composite genera.

Critique of the 'Primarily' Condition in Species Definition
Author critiques the addition of 'primarily' and presents al-Sayyid's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Sayyid al-Sharif's objection to adding 'qawlan awwaliyyan' to the definition of species and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
Adding 'primarily' to species definition backfires by excluding species from being species of distant genera.

The passage includes a critical note on the definition of relative species. Some logicians added the condition 'primarily' to exclude subspecies, but al-Sayyid al-Sharif argues this condition causes more problems than it solves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition being critiqued
The condition 'qawlan awwaliyyan' (primarily) in the definition of species, intended to exclude subspecies (al-sinf).
The passage: 'قَوْلًا أَوَّلِيًّا، خرج به الصنف'
2
Understand the first objection
If 'primarily' means directly, then man would not be a species of body (a distant genus), but man is called species of species. So the condition is too restrictive.
3
Understand the second objection
Species and genus are correlatives. If 'primarily' applies to species, it must also apply to genus, making distant genera not genera—an absurdity.
4
Identify the proposed solution
Omit 'primarily' and exclude subspecies by another condition: 'genus is predicated of it and of other things'.
The passage ends with the preferred definition.

The Hierarchy of Genus and Species
Author defines hierarchical ordering of genus and species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any universal term as highest, lowest, or intermediate in a hierarchy of genus and species, using the criteria of generality and specificity.

Core Idea
Genus and species are relative: one term can be both a genus and a species depending on perspective.

In logic, universals like 'animal' and 'human' do not exist in isolation—they form ordered hierarchies. This lesson unpacks the key terms: highest (أَعَمّ), lowest (أَخَصّ), and intermediate (مُتَوَسِّط) genera/species. Understanding this ordering is essential for constructing precise definitions and avoiding errors in reasoning about categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms in the hierarchy
Look at a chain like 'body' > 'animal' > 'human' > 'Zayd'. Each term can be a genus for what is below and a species for what is above.
The text uses 'species' (نوع) in a broad sense, including individuals when necessary.
2
Find the most general term (أَعَمّ الكُلّ)
This is the term at the top of the hierarchy, predicable of everything below. In the example, 'body' is the most general. The passage says it can be either a genus or a species (if nothing is above it).
3
Find the most specific term (أَخَصّ الكُلّ)
This is the lowest term, below which nothing else falls. It could be an infima species like 'human' or even an individual like 'Zayd'. The text clarifies that it may be called a species or a genus (if it is the most specific but still has individuals under it).
4
Classify any term between them as intermediate (مُتَوَسِّط)
A term that is more general than some and more specific than others, like 'animal' above 'human' but below 'body'. The passage states that the intermediate is identified by being more general than some and more specific than some.
5
Apply the principle 'genus-ness by generality'
The text explains that a term's status as a genus comes from its generality. A term is a genus for everything it is predicated of (its species). This principle allows you to determine the hierarchy.
This is consistent with the traditional Porphyrian tree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجنس (أَوْ مُرَتَّب) ، (وَهُوَ ) ، أي النوع (كَالْجِنْسِ، إِمَّا مُفْرَدٌ ، بأن لا يكون فوقه وتحته نوع ، وهو ضده، فيكون أنواع وأجناس مرتبة، (وَأَخَصُ الْكُلَّ السَّافِلُ) نوعا كان أو جنسا، (وَأَعَمُّ الْكُلِّ الْعَالي)، نوعا كان أو جنسا، (وَالْأَعَمُّ) من البعض أَوِ الْأَخَصُّ من البعض (الْمُتَوَسِّطُ)، جنسا كان أو نوعا ; لأَنَّ الْجِنْسِيَّةَ بِاعْتِبارِ الْعُمُومِ) ؛ فإن الأعم المقول عليها وعلى حقيقة
And genus (or ordered), and it (i.e., the species) is like the genus; either single, meaning that there is no species above or below it, and it is its opposite, so there are ordered species and genera. (And the most specific of all is the lowest) whether species or genus, (and the most general of all is the highest) whether species or genus, (and the more general than some or the more specific than some is the intermediate) whether genus or species; because genus-ness is by consideration of generality; for the most general is said of it and of the reality...
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage defines the hierarchical ordering of genus (jins) and species (nawʻ). It begins by stating that genus can be 'ordered' (murattab), meaning it can be arranged in a series. The species, like the genus, can be 'single' (mufrad), having no species above or below it, which is its opposite. Thus, genera and species form ordered chains. The text then introduces three relative terms: 'the most specific of all' (akhaṣṣ al-kull) is the lowest, whether it is a species or genus; 'the most general of all' (aʻamm al-kull) is the highest; and 'the intermediate' (al-mutawassiṭ) is what is more general than some and more specific than others. This classification is based on the principle that genus-ness is by consideration of generality ('umūm). The most general term applies to itself and to what is under it. The passage essentially lays out the relative hierarchy in the Porphyrian tree, where each node is a genus relative to what is below and a species relative to what is above.

Key Terms
الجنس
النوع
المتوسط
السافل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a hierarchy where 'animal' is above 'human' and 'human' is above 'Zayd', what is 'human' in relation to 'animal' and 'Zayd'?
A) The most general.
B) The most specific.
C) The intermediate.
D) A single genus.

Key Takeaway
The relative positions of genus and species are determined by generality: the most general is the highest, the most specific is the lowest, and anything in between is intermediate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following hierarchy in Islamic jurisprudence: 'Ruling (ḥukm)' > 'Wājib' > 'Prayer (ṣalāh)'. Identify which term is the most general, which is the most specific, and which is the intermediate. Explain your reasoning.
?

The Relative Hierarchy of Genus and Species
Author explains the relative hierarchy of jins and naw' based on generality and specificity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relative positions of genus and species in any universal hierarchy and determine whether a universal is a highest genus, lowest species, or intermediate.

Core Idea
Genus and species are relative designations based on generality or specificity within a hierarchy.

In the Islamic logical tradition, the terms 'genus' (jins) and 'species' (naw') are not fixed labels attached to static categories. Instead, they are relational: a universal is a genus relative to what is more specific, and a species relative to what is more general. This lesson unpacks this relativity, explaining how the same universal can function as both genus and species depending on direction, and introduces the concepts of the highest genus (jins al-ajnas) and lowest species (naw' al-anwa'). Understanding this hierarchy is essential for constructing sound definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the principle of relativity
The passage states that genus-ness (jinsiyya) is based on generality, and species-ness (naw'iyya) on specificity. A universal that is more general than another and predicated of it is its genus; a universal that is more specific than another and predicated of its genus is its species. This means the same universal can be a genus in one relation and a species in another.
For example, 'body' is a species relative to 'substance' but a genus relative to 'animal'.
2
Identify the three positions in the hierarchy
The author describes three levels: the lowest (most specific) which has nothing below it, the highest (most general) which has nothing above it, and the intermediate which is both more general than what is below and more specific than what is above.
These correspond to the lowest species (naw' al-safil), the highest genus (jins al-'ali), and the intermediate universals (mutawassit).
3
Understand the terminology for extremes
The lowest species is called 'species of species' (naw' al-anwa'), and the highest genus is called 'genus of genera' (jins al-ajnas). The highest genus is also said to be constitutive (muqawwim) of all below it because it enters into their essence.
The phrase 'laff wa nashr ghayr murattab' indicates that these names are mentioned in an unarranged order.
4
Apply the concept to a concrete Islamic example
Consider the classification of existents in Islamic philosophy: 'Substance' (jawhar) is the highest genus, 'Body' (jism) is a species relative to substance but a genus relative to 'Animal', 'Animal' (hayawan) is a species relative to body but a genus relative to 'Human', and 'Human' (insan) is the lowest species in this chain.
This example shows how each term shifts role depending on the level of comparison.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجنس (أَوْ مُرَتَّب) وَهُوَ أي النوع كَالْجِنْسِ إِمَّا مُفْرَدٌ بأن لا يكون فوقه وتحته نوع وهو ضده فيكون أنواع وأجناس مرتبة وَأَخَصُ الْكُلَّ السَّافِلُ نوعا كان أو جنسا وَأَعَمُّ الْكُلِّ الْعَالي نوعا كان أو جنسا وَالْأَعَمُّ من البعض أَوِ الْأَخَصُّ من البعض الْمُتَوَسِّطُ جنسا كان أو نوعا لأَنَّ الْجِنْسِيَّةَ بِاعْتِبَارِ الْعُمُومِ فإن الأعم المقول عليها وعلى حقيقة أخرى يكون جنسا وَالنَّوْعِيَّةَ بِاعْتِبَارِ الْخُصُوص فإن الأخص المقول عليه وعلى غيره الجنس يكون نوعا. يُسَمَّى النَّوْعُ السَّافِلُ نَوْعَ الْأَنْوَاعِ وَالْجِنْسُ الْعَالي جِنْسَ الْأَجْنَاسِ ويسمى مقوما له أيضا لف ونشر غير مرتب.
And genus (or arranged). And it, i.e. the species, is like the genus: either simple, in that there is no species above it and below it, and it is its opposite, so there are arranged species and genera. And the most specific of all is the lowest, whether species or genus. And the most general of all is the highest, whether species or genus. And what is more general than some or more specific than some is the intermediate, whether genus or species. Because genus-ness is with respect to generality: the more general that is predicated of it and of another reality is genus. And species-ness is with respect to specificity: the more specific that is predicated of it and of another genus is species. The lowest species is called the species of species, and the highest genus is called the genus of genera, and it is also called the constitutive of it, an unarranged wrapping and unfolding.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage expands on the relative nature of genus and species in the hierarchy of universals. The author explains that genus-ness (jinsiyya) is determined by generality: a universal that is more general, predicated of itself and another entity, functions as a genus. Conversely, species-ness (naw'iyya) is determined by specificity: a universal that is more specific, predicated of itself and another genus, functions as a species. This relativity means that the same universal can be a genus relative to what is below it and a species relative to what is above it. The passage identifies three positions: the highest genus (al-jins al-'ali), which has no genus above it; the lowest species (al-naw' al-safil), which has no species below it; and intermediate universals (mutawassit) that are both genera and species depending on direction. The lowest species is called 'species of species' (naw' al-anwa'), and the highest genus is called 'genus of genera' (jins al-ajnas), which is also said to be constitutive (muqawwim) of all below it. The phrase 'laff wa nashr ghayr murattab' indicates that the names are given in an unarranged order, meaning the text presents them without sequential pairing.

Definition of Differentia and the No-Genus Rule
Author defines al-fasl and states the no-genus condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the differentia (al-fasl) among the five universals and apply the condition that a term without a genus cannot have a differentia.

Core Idea
Differentia requires a genus; existence defies definition.

The third universal, al-fasl (differentia), is what distinguishes a species within its genus. However, not everything can be differentiated — only things that fall under a genus. In this passage, the author defines al-fasl and then warns that transcendentals like existence (al-wujud) have no genus and thus no differentia, preventing circular definitions.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of al-fasl
Find the phrase that defines the differentia. It is a universal predicated in answer to 'what thing is it in its essence?' (ayy shay' huwa fi jawharihi).
This distinguishes it from the genus, which answers 'what is it?' (ma huwa).
2
Understand the exclusion of 'ma huwa'
The author clarifies that technical usage restricts 'ayy shay' to something not said in answer to 'what is it'. This excludes the definition (hadd) and the genus itself from being differentiae.
The differentia never answers the question 'what is it?' – that belongs to the genus or species.
3
Analyze the no-genus condition
The passage states that what has no genus has no differentia. The justification is a conditional: if it had a differentia without a genus, the two parts (the supposed genus and differentia) would both be differentiae, making union impossible.
This implies a valid modus tollens argument: if there were a differentia without a genus, absurdity follows; therefore, no differentia without genus.
4
Identify the example of existence
Existence (al-wujud) is given as an example of something with no genus. It is a transcendental that cuts across all categories.
The gloss reinforces that existence cannot have a genus because that would require existence to be qualified by existence, a circularity.
5
Examine the gloss's argument
The gloss adds: 'Existence has no genus; otherwise it would be qualified by existence.' This shows the circularity: if existence had a genus, that genus would have to apply to existence, but existence is already the most general.
This is a succinct argument reinforcing the matn's claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْمَبْحَثُ الثَّالِثُ : الْفَصْلُ الكلي الثَّالِثُ الْفَضْلُ وَهُوَ الْمَقُولُ أي كلي قيل وحمل في جَوَابِ أَيُّ شَيْءٍ هُوَ فِي جَوْهَرِهِ وأي شي وإن كان طالبا لمطلق المميز اللغة لكن الاصطلاح وقع على مميز لا يكون مقولا في جواب ما هو فخرج الحد والجنس وَمَا لَا جِنْسَ لَهُ كَالْوُجُودِ لَا فَضْلَ لَهُ وإلا لكان الجزءان فصلين فيكونان متحصلين فلا يمكن الاتحاد بينهما بحسب فتأمل فيه. قال في الحاشية: الوجود لا جنس له وإلا فإما أن يتصف بالوجود.
The third investigation: the differentia. The third universal is the differentia, and it is the predicate, i.e. a universal that is said and predicated in answer to 'what thing is it in its essence?', and 'what thing'—although it seeks, in language, an absolute distinguisher—technical usage has settled on a distinguisher that is not predicated in answer to 'what is it', so the definition (hadd) and genus are excluded. And that which has no genus, like existence, has no differentia. Otherwise the two parts would be two differentiae, thus they would be subsumed, and union between them would not be possible. So reflect on this. He said in the gloss: Existence has no genus; otherwise it would either be qualified by existence.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage begins the third section (mabhath) on the third universal: al-fasl (differentia). The differentia is defined as a universal that is predicated in answer to 'what thing is it in its essence?' (ayy shay' huwa fi jawharihi). The explanation clarifies that although the interrogative 'ayy shay' generally seeks any distinguisher, in technical logical terminology it specifically refers to a distinguisher that is not said in answer to 'what is it?' (ma huwa). This exclusion removes the definition (hadd, which combines genus and differentia) and the genus itself. The passage then states a key principle: that which has no genus, such as existence (al-wujud), has no differentia. This is because a differentia always distinguishes a species under a genus; without a genus, there is no basis for differentiation. The text warns that if it were otherwise, the two parts (supposedly the genus and differentia) would both be differentiae, leading to subsumption and impossibility of union. The gloss (hashiya) is quoted to reinforce that existence cannot have a genus, because if it did, it would need to be qualified by existence, leading to circularity. This passage guards against the misconception that everything can be defined by genus and differentia; transcendentals like existence are exceptions.

Key Takeaway
A differentia can only exist for terms that have a genus; transcendentals like existence escape definition by genus and differentia.

Close and Distant Differentia
Author distinguishes close and distant differentia with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between close and distant differentia and identify them in definitions from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A differentia's closeness depends on the genus it distinguishes within.

Differentia (fasl) is what makes a species what it is. But not all differentiae are equal: some are close, directly separating the species from others under the same genus; others are distant, separating it from a wider group. Knowing this helps you analyze definitions in fiqh, kalam, and usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the species and its proximate genus
Take the species being defined. Ask: what is the immediate genus it falls under? For example, 'human' falls under 'animal' as proximate genus.
2
Locate the differentia
The differentia is what distinguishes the species from other species under that same genus. For human, it is 'rational'. That is a close differentia because it specifies within 'animal'.
3
Now consider a more remote genus
If you step back to an even higher genus, like 'body', what distinguishes human from other bodies? 'Sentient' (or 'living') is a distant differentia relative to 'body'.
4
Check the source's rule
Notice: the distant differentia for the species is close for its own genus. 'Sentient' is close for 'animal' (distinguishes it from plants and minerals), but distant for 'human'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكليات الخمس
الْفَصْلِ الْقَريب فَقَريبٌ، كالناطق للإنسان، أو مميز عن مشاركات الجنس الْفَصْلِ الْبَعِيدِ فَبَعِيدٌ، كالحساس للإنسان، وكل فصل بعيد لماهية فهو قريب لجنس من أجناسه، وله نسبة إلى النوع بالتقويم والدخول فيه، فيسمى مقومًا، وكل مقوم للعالي، نوعا كان أو جنسا فهو مقوم للمسائل: لأن جزء الجزء جزء، ولا عكس كليا، أي ليس كل مقوم للسافل مقوما للعالي؛ إذ ربما يكون مقسما له، وله نسبة إلى الجنس بالتقسيم إلى النوعين، فيسمى مقسما، وكل مقسم للسافل مقسم للعالي: إذ مقسم الأخص مقسم الأعم، ولا عكس.
Chapter on the Five Universals. The close differentia is close, like 'rational' for human, or distinguishing from the participants of the genus. The distant differentia is distant, like 'sentient' for human. Every distant differentia for a quiddity is close for one of its genera. It has a relation to the species by way of constituting and entering into it, so it is called a constitutor. Every constitutor for a higher entity, whether species or genus, is a constitutor for its parts, because a part of a part is a part. But not universally, i.e., not every constitutor for a lower entity is a constitutor for a higher one, since sometimes it is a divider of it. It has a relation to the genus by way of dividing into the two species, so it is called a divider. Every divider for a lower entity is a divider for a higher one: since the divider of the more specific is a divider of the more general. Not vice versa.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage defines the close and distant differentia and explains their two fundamental relations: taqwim (constitution) and taqsim (division). The close differentia, exemplified by 'rational' for humans, distinguishes the species from others under the immediate genus. The distant differentia, like 'sentient', distinguishes from beings under a more remote genus. Every distant differentia for a species is close relative to some higher genus. The differentia's relation to the species is called taqwim: it enters into the definition of the species and constitutes it, hence it is called muqawwim. Any muqawwim for a higher entity (species or genus) is also a muqawwim for its parts—since a part of a part is a part. However, the converse does not hold: some muqawwim for a lower entity are not muqawwim for a higher entity because they may serve as muqassim (divider) for that higher entity. The differentia's relation to the genus is taqsim: it divides the genus into species, so it is called muqassim. Any muqassim for a lower entity is also a muqassim for a higher one, because dividing the more specific implies dividing the more general, but not vice versa. These distinctions are crucial for understanding how differentia functions in definition.

Key Terms
الفصل القريب
الفصل البعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Sentient
B) Rational
C) Corporeal
D) Living

Key Takeaway
A differentia can be close or distant depending on the genus level; the same differentia may be close for one genus and distant for another.

Taqwim and Taqsim: Two Functions of Differentia
Author explains taqwim and taqsim as relations of differentia to species and genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two functions of differentia—taqwim (constitution) and taqsim (division)—and apply them to definitions in Islamic law and theology.

Core Idea
Differentia both constitutes the species and divides the genus.

Every differentia has two faces: it enters into the definition of the species (taqwim) and it splits the genus into its species (taqsim). Understanding this dual role is essential for constructing and analyzing definitions in Islamic sciences like fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Understand taqwim (constitution)
Taqwim means the differentia constitutes the essence of the species. It is part of the definition (hadd). For example, 'rational' is a muqawwim (constitutor) of human: without it, the species would not be what it is.
2
Apply the rule upward
The passage says: every muqawwim for a higher entity (species or genus) is a muqawwim for its parts. If 'rational' constitutes 'human', it also constitutes 'individual human' because part of a part is a part.
But not every muqawwim for a lower entity is muqawwim for a higher one.
3
Understand taqsim (division)
Taqsim means the differentia divides the genus into species. 'Rational' divides the genus 'animal' into rational and irrational. So it is a muqassim (divider) of 'animal'.
4
Apply the rule downward
Every muqassim for a lower entity is a muqassim for a higher one. If 'rational' divides 'human' (species) into subclasses, it also divides 'animal' (genus) because dividing the more specific implies dividing the more general.
But not vice versa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكليات الخمس
الْفَصْلِ الْقَريب فَقَريبٌ، كالناطق للإنسان، أو مميز عن مشاركات الجنس الْفَصْلِ الْبَعِيدِ فَبَعِيدٌ، كالحساس للإنسان، وكل فصل بعيد لماهية فهو قريب لجنس من أجناسه، وله نسبة إلى النوع بالتقويم والدخول فيه، فيسمى مقومًا، وكل مقوم للعالي، نوعا كان أو جنسا فهو مقوم للمسائل: لأن جزء الجزء جزء، ولا عكس كليا، أي ليس كل مقوم للسافل مقوما للعالي؛ إذ ربما يكون مقسما له، وله نسبة إلى الجنس بالتقسيم إلى النوعين، فيسمى مقسما، وكل مقسم للسافل مقسم للعالي: إذ مقسم الأخص مقسم الأعم، ولا عكس.

It has a relation to the genus by way of dividing into the two species, so it is called a divider. This passage defines the close and distant differentia and explains their two fundamental relations: taqwim (constitution) and taqsim (division). The close differentia, exemplified by 'rational' for humans, distinguishes the species from others under the immediate genus. The distant differentia, like 'sentient', distinguishes from beings under a more remote genus. Every distant differentia for a species is close relative to some higher genus. The Differentia as Complete Cause of Genus
Author presents the causality claim and a counterargument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the claim that the differentia is a complete cause of the genus and identify the counterargument.

Core Idea
Differentia determines genus, but whether it is a complete cause is debated.

In this passage, the philosophers assert that the differentia is the complete cause of the genus's determination. However, later scholars challenge this, arguing that the differentia and genus are distinct as matter and form, so the causality is only incomplete. This debate sets the stage for four derived rules that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Find the claim that the differentia is a complete cause (illa tamma) for the genus. Note that the genus is described as indeterminate and oscillating, and it becomes determinate only through the differentia.
2
Extract the objection
Later scholars say the differentia and genus are distinct as matter and form, so causality holds but only as an incomplete cause (illa naqisa). Understand why this weakens subsequent derivations.
3
Understand the consequence
The shift from complete to incomplete cause affects the validity of the four issues that follow. The text says some derivations are not valid if the cause is incomplete.
4
Evaluate the strength of the objection
Consider whether the objection is valid. Does the analogy of matter and form fully capture the relationship? What does the phrase 'there is something in it' indicate?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الحكماء: الجنس أمر مبهم، متزلزل بين أن يكون هذه الحقيقة أو تلك، لا يتحصل إلا بالفصل، ولهذا يتحد معه، فهو علة تامة (الذا) أي لتحصله، فلا يرد أنهما متحدان، فكيف العلية؟ وفيه ما فيه.
قال بعض الأجلة من المتأخرين: إنهما متغايران حال كونهما مادة وصورة، فالعلية صحيحة. هذا مع التوقف على التلازم لا يفيد إلا علية ناقصة، فلا يصح بعض التفريعات الآتية.
مسائل أربعة:
فلا يكون فصل الجنس جنسا للفصل، بأن يتركب ماهية من جزئين بينهما عموم من وجه فيكون كل منهما جنسا مشتركا فيها وفي ماهية أخرى، وفصلا مميزا لها بالنسبة إلى ماهية لا يوجد فيها، وإلا لزم الدور، وقد تقدم ما فيه.
قال بعض الأجلة قد تقرر أن الجنس عرض عام للفصل، فإذا كان فصل الجنس جنسا للفصل يلزم أن يكون كل منهما عرضا عاما للآخر، وهو ظاهر الفساد، ولا يخفى ما فيه؛ فإن الكليين الذين بينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه كل منهما عرض عام للآخر، فلا فساد فيه، ولعل لكلامه وجها لست أحصله.
ولا يكون لشيء واحد فصلان قريبان، وإلا لزم توارد العلل المستقلة على معلول واحد، وإن كان في إعطاء التحصل، وربما يقرر بأنه إن كفى كل منهما في تحصله نوعا فيلزم الاستغناء عن الذاتي، وإلا فيكون مجموعهما فصلا واحدا، وهذا إنما يتم لو كان الفصل عبارة عن المميز التام، وإلا فلا يخفى ما فيه.
ولا يقوم الفصل إلا نوعا واحدا، إذا لم يكن اعتباريا، وإلا لكان الشيء علة لأمرين، وهما جنساه، ولا يقارن إلا جنسا واحدا، فيحصله.
The philosophers said: The genus is an indeterminate matter, oscillating between being this reality or that, not becoming determinate except by the differentia, and therefore it unites with it, so it is a complete cause (al-dha?) i.e., for its determination, so it is not objected that they are united, so how is causality? There is something in it. Some later eminent scholars said: They are distinct as matter and form, so the causality is correct. This, with the condition of concomitance, only yields an incomplete cause, so some of the following derivations are not valid. Four issues: (1) The differentia of a genus cannot be a genus for the differentia, because a quiddity composed of two parts with commonality in some respect would each be a common genus in it and in another quiddity, and a distinguishing differentia for it relative to a quiddity in which it does not exist; otherwise circularity would follow, and what has preceded is sufficient. Some later eminent scholar said: It has been established that the genus is a general attribute for the differentia, so if the differentia of a genus were a genus for the differentia, each would be a general attribute for the other, which is evidently corrupt. But it is not hidden that there is something in it: for the two universals that have commonality and specificity in some respect, each is a general attribute for the other, so there is no corruption. Perhaps his statement has an aspect I do not grasp. (2) A single thing cannot have two proximate differentiae; otherwise independent causes would converge on one effect, even if in giving determination. It may be argued that if each suffices for its determination as a type, then independence from the essential would follow; otherwise their total would be one differentia. This is only valid if the differentia is the complete distinguisher; otherwise it is not hidden. (3) The differentia constitutes only one species, if it is not accidental; otherwise a thing would be a cause for two things, which are its two genera. (4) It accompanies only one genus, thus determining it.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage discusses the causal relationship between differentia and genus, and derives four rules. The philosophers assert that the genus is indeterminate and becomes determinate only through the differentia, so the differentia is a complete cause for the genus. Some later scholars object that they are distinct as matter and form, so causality holds, but this only gives incomplete causality, which weakens subsequent derivations. Four issues (masa'il arba') are then presented: (1) The differentia of a genus cannot itself be a genus for that differentia—because that would create a circular overlap where each would be a genus for the other, which is argued to be corrupt. However, a counterpoint is noted: two universals with partial overlap can each be a general attribute for the other, so the corruption is not obvious. (2) A single species cannot have two proximate differentiae; otherwise two independent causes would produce one effect. If each suffices, one is superfluous; if not, they combine as one differentia—but this assumes differentia is a complete distinguisher. (3) A differentia constitutes only one species, unless it is accidental (i'tibari). (4) A differentia accompanies only one genus, thereby determining that genus. These issues illustrate the rigorous analysis of logical relations in definition.

Key Terms
الجنس (al-jins): The genus, an indeterminate matter that becomes determinate only through the differentia. [Note: No significant shift; standard usage in Mantiq.]
الفصل (al-fasl): The differentia that determines the genus and is said to be a complete cause for it. [Note: The claim that differentia is a cause (illa) for genus is debated; later logicians may not emphasize causality.]
العلة التامة (al-'illa al-tamma): The complete cause that fully determines its effect; here attributed to the differentia in relation to the genus. [Note: In other contexts, 'illa tamma' may refer to a cause with all conditions fulfilled; here it is used analogically.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later scholars, why is the causality of the differentia only incomplete?
A) Because the differentia is not the only cause.
B) Because the differentia and genus are distinct as matter and form.
C) Because the genus cannot be determined.
D) Because the differentia is accidental.

Key Takeaway
The relationship between differentia and genus is causal but debated as complete or incomplete, affecting the precision of definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the definition of 'insan', identify the genus and differentia. Then argue whether the differentia is a complete cause of the genus's determination based on the criteria discussed.
?
Write a brief response to the later scholars' objection, explaining why the causality might still be considered complete despite the distinction.

Issue 1: Differentia Cannot Be a Genus for the Differentia
Author presents the first issue and a scholarly exchange

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the differentia of a genus cannot also be a genus for that differentia, and recognize the counterargument.

Core Idea
The differentia and genus must not be mutually predicable genera, else circularity arises.

The first derived issue asks whether the differentia of a genus could itself serve as a genus for that differentia. The philosophers argue that this leads to circularity, but a later scholar points out that two universals with partial overlap can each be genera for the other. This issue tests the precise relationship between genus and differentia.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Read the statement: 'The differentia of a genus cannot be a genus for the differentia.' Consider what would happen if it were allowed.
2
Identify the argument against it
The argument says that if allowed, a quiddity composed of two parts with commonality in some respect would lead to each being a common genus, and circularity (dawr) would follow.
3
Examine the counterargument
A later scholar objects: it has been established that the genus is a general attribute for the differentia. If the differentia were a genus for the differentia, each would be a general attribute for the other, which is evidently corrupt. But he notes that two universals with commonality in some respect can each be a general attribute for the other, so it may not be corrupt.
4
Evaluate the debate
Consider which position is stronger. Does the counterargument successfully refute the initial prohibition? What does the phrase 'perhaps his statement has an aspect I do not grasp' indicate?

Source Text

قال بعض الأجلة من المتأخرين: إنهما متغايران حال كونهما مادة وصورة، فالعلية صحيحة. هذا مع التوقف على التلازم لا يفيد إلا علية ناقصة، فلا يصح بعض التفريعات الآتية.

الجنس (al-jins): The genus, here discussed as a general attribute for the differentia. [Note: Same as earlier.]
الفصل (al-fasl): The differentia, which is said to be a general attribute for the genus? Actually, the passage discusses the reverse relation.
الدور (al-dawr): Circularity, a logical fallacy where a term is defined in terms of itself. [Note: Standard usage.]

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given against the differentia being a genus for the differentia?
A) It leads to an infinite regress.
B) It leads to circularity (dawr).
C) It makes the differentia accidental.
D) It confuses two different definitions.

Key Takeaway
The relationship between genus and differentia is asymmetric to avoid circularity.

?

Issue 2: No Two Proximate Differentiae for One Species
Author presents the second issue

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to justify why a single species cannot have two proximate differentiae, considering the nuance about complete distinguisher.

Core Idea
Two independent causes cannot produce one effect; thus one species has one differentia.

The second issue addresses whether a species can have two proximate differentiae. The philosophers say no, because each would be an independent cause, leading to either redundancy or the need to combine them into one. The reasoning hinges on the concept of the differentia as the complete distinguisher.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
The text states: 'A single thing cannot have two proximate differentiae.' Understand what 'proximate' (qariban) means in this context.
2
Understand the reasoning
Two differentiae would mean two independent causes converging on one effect (the species). This is problematic because if each suffices, one is unnecessary; if neither suffices, they combine to form one differentia.
3
Examine the condition
The argument only works if the differentia is the complete distinguisher (al-mumayyiz al-tamm). If it is not complete, the reasoning may not hold.
4
Apply the rule
Test a definition: e.g., 'human' defined as 'rational animal'. Could it have two differentiae like 'rational' and 'mortal'? Explain why or why not based on this issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المميز التام (al-mumayyiz al-tamm): The complete distinguisher, which is assumed for the argument against two differentiae.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument against two proximate differentiae for one species?
A) It would make the species too general.

D) It would violate the rule of one genus per species.

Key Takeaway
A species cannot have two proximate differentiae because it would imply multiple independent causes for one effect.

?

Issue 3: A Differentia Constitutes Only One Species
Author presents the third issue

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a differentia constitutes only one species unless it is accidental.

Core Idea
A differentia is specific to one species; otherwise it would cause multiple species from one cause.

The third issue states that each differentia constitutes exactly one species. If it could constitute two, it would be a cause for two distinct species, which is impossible for a single cause. However, accidental differentiae (i'tibari) may be exempt from this rule.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
The text says: 'The differentia constitutes only one species, if it is not accidental.' Identify the condition.
2
Reason why
If a differentia constituted two species, it would be a cause for two distinct things, which is problematic because a single cause cannot produce two different effects.
3
Consider the exception
If the differentia is accidental (i'tibari), it might not be bound by this rule. What does 'accidental' mean in this context?
4
Apply the rule
Give an example of a differentia that uniquely defines a species. For instance, 'rational' for human, 'neighing' for horse. Check if it constitutes only one species.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Four issues: (1) The differentia of a genus cannot be a genus for the differentia, because a quiddity composed of two parts with commonality in some respect would each be a common genus in it and in another quiddity, and a distinguishing differentia for it relative to a quiddity in which it does not exist; otherwise circularity would follow, and what has preceded is sufficient. Some later eminent scholar said: It has been established that the genus is a general attribute for the differentia, so if the differentia of a genus were a genus for the differentia, each would be a general attribute for the other, which is evidently corrupt. But it is not hidden that there is something in it: for the two universals that have commonality and specificity in some respect, each is a general attribute for the other, so there is no corruption. Perhaps his statement has an aspect I do not grasp. (2) A single thing cannot have two proximate differentiae; otherwise independent causes would converge on one effect, even if in giving determination. It may be argued that if each suffices for its determination as a type, then independence from the essential would follow; otherwise their total would be one differentia. This is only valid if the differentia is the complete distinguisher; otherwise it is not hidden. (3) The differentia constitutes only one species, if it is not accidental; otherwise a thing would be a cause for two things, which are its two genera. (4) It accompanies only one genus, thus determining it.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage discusses the causal relationship between differentia and genus, and derives four rules. The philosophers assert that the genus is indeterminate and becomes determinate only through the differentia, so the differentia is a complete cause for the genus. Some later scholars object that they are distinct as matter and form, so causality holds, but this only gives incomplete causality, which weakens subsequent derivations. Four issues (masa'il arba') are then presented: (1) The differentia of a genus cannot itself be a genus for that differentia—because that would create a circular overlap where each would be a genus for the other, which is argued to be corrupt. However, a counterpoint is noted: two universals with partial overlap can each be a general attribute for the other, so the corruption is not obvious. (2) A single species cannot have two proximate differentiae; otherwise two independent causes would produce one effect. If each suffices, one is superfluous; if not, they combine as one differentia—but this assumes differentia is a complete distinguisher. (3) A differentia constitutes only one species, unless it is accidental (i'tibari). (4) A differentia accompanies only one genus, thereby determining that genus. These issues illustrate the rigorous analysis of logical relations in definition.

Key Terms
اعتباري (i'tibari): Accidental or conventional, as opposed to essential.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, a differentia constitutes how many species?
A) At least two
B) Only one
C) Unlimited
D) Depends on the genus

Key Takeaway
A differentia is essential to one species; accidental differentiae may be exceptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Define 'jinn' as a species; what differentia would you assign to ensure it constitutes only one species?
?
Critique a definition where one differentia is claimed for two species (e.g., 'rational' for humans and perhaps for angels). Use the reasoning from this issue.

Issue 4: A Differentia Accompanies Only One Genus
Author presents the fourth issue

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a differentia accompanies only one genus.

Core Idea
A differentia determines one specific genus and cannot be shared across genera.

The fourth and final issue is complementary to the third: just as one differentia constitutes one species, it also accompanies only one genus. The differentia gives determination to that genus alone, ensuring the hierarchy of classification is maintained.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
The text says: 'It accompanies only one genus, thus determining it.' Understand what 'accompanies' means.
2
Reason why
If a differentia accompanied two genera, it would have to determine both, which is problematic because it would be a cause for two distinct genera. This is similar to issue 3.
3
Relate to issue 3
Issue 3 says one differentia for one species; here it says one differentia for one genus. Together they ensure a one-to-one mapping in the definitional chain.
4
Test with examples
In the definition 'hayawan natiq', the differentia 'natiq' accompanies only the genus 'hayawan', not 'jism' or 'maddah'. Verify this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قال بعض الأجلة من المتأخرين: إنهما متغايران حال كونهما مادة وصورة، فالعلية صحيحة. هذا مع التوقف على التلازم لا يفيد إلا علية ناقصة، فلا يصح بعض التفريعات الآتية.

There is something in it. Some later eminent scholars said: They are distinct as matter and form, so the causality is correct. General-Special Overlap: Genus and Differentia Interchange
Author explains mutual genus-differentia relation under general-special overlap.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when two universals have general-special overlap (عموم من وجه) and explain why in that case each can serve as both genus and differentia for the other without circularity.

Core Idea
When two universals overlap partially, they can each be the other’s genus and differentia — no infinite regress.

In the five universals, genus and differentia are normally distinct, with the genus being broader. But what happens when two universals share some instances but not others? This lesson explores the special case of 'general-special overlap' (عموم من وجه), where each universal can act as a genus for the other and as a differentia distinguishing the other from a third quiddity. The passage also addresses a common objection about circularity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two universals in an example of general-special overlap
Pick a pair like 'animal' and 'rational'. They overlap in humans but not elsewhere. Ensure neither is fully contained in the other.
e.g., animal includes rational and non-rational; rational includes animals (humans) and possibly angels.
2
Verify each is a common genus for the other and for another quiddity
Animal is genus for human and horse; rational is genus for human and angel. So each universal is a genus encompassing the other's species plus another.
3
Check the objection that each would be a common accident for the other
The objector says if differentia of the genus becomes genus for differentia, then both become common accidents, which seems corrupt. Note the rebuttal: in general-special overlap, this is exactly the case and it is not corrupt.
The text says the objection is unfounded.
4
Confirm that there is no circularity or regress
Circularity (دور) would occur if each were genus and differentia for the same quiddity. But here each genus-differentia relation is relative to different quiddities, so no recursion.
The author states such a circularity would require an infinite regress and is avoided by the overlap structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جزئين بينهما عموم من وجه فيكون كل منهما جنسا مشتركا فيها وفي ماهية أخرى ، وفصلا مميزا لها بالنسبة إلى ماهية لا يوجد فيها ، وإلا لزم الدور، وقد تقدم ما فيه. قال بعض الأجلة قد تقرر أن الجنس عرض عام للفصل، فإذا كان فصل الجنس جنسا للفصل يلزم أن يكون كل منهما عرضا عاما للآخر، وهو ظاهر الفساد، ولا يخفى ما فيه ؛ فإن الكليين الذين بينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه كل منهما عرض عام للآخر، فلا فساد فيه، ولعل لكلامه وجها لست أحصله .
Two things between which there is general-special overlap, so each of them is a common genus for it and for another quiddity, and a distinguishing differentia from a quiddity in which it is not found; otherwise an infinite regress would occur, and its discussion has preceded. A certain eminent figure said: It has been established that the genus is a common accident for the differentia. So if the differentia of the genus were a genus for the differentia, then each would be a common accident for the other, which is clearly corrupt, and what is in it is not hidden. For the two universals which have general-special overlap are each a common accident for the other, so there is no corruption in it. Perhaps his statement has a face that I do not grasp.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage discusses the relationship between two universals that have a general-special overlap (such as animal and human as differentia). It begins by defining how each can serve as a common genus for the other and as a differentia distinguishing it from another quiddity. The commentator then reports a critique from a certain eminent figure who argues that if the differentia of the genus becomes a genus for the differentia, then each would be a common accident for the other, which seems invalid. However, the commentary refutes this by noting that in cases of general-special overlap, each universal is indeed a common accident for the other, so the critique is unfounded. The passage ends with a note of uncertainty about the objector's intended meaning.
