Subtypes of Necessary Accidents: Necessary of Essence vs Necessary of Existence
Author further classifies necessary accident into necessary of essence and necessary of mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the necessary of the essence (lāzim al-māhiyya) and the necessary of mental existence (lāzim al-wujūd al-dhihnī).

Core Idea
The necessary accident further divides into what is inseparable from the essence absolutely vs what is inseparable only in mental existence.

After dividing accidents into necessary and separable, the author delves deeper into the necessary. Some necessary attributes are so fundamental that they cannot be separated from the essence in any state, like the potentiality (imkān) of a contingent being. Others are only inseparable when the essence exists in the mind, like quiddity itself. This distinction is crucial for understanding secondary intelligibles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two subtypes of necessary
Necessary of the essence (lāzim al-māhiyya) and necessary of mental existence (lāzim al-wujūd al-dhihnī).
2
Understand necessary of the essence
Separation is absolutely impossible, whether in external or mental existence, due to a cause or self-evident necessity.
3
Note examples of necessary of the essence
Absolute existence (wujūd muṭlaq) and potentiality (imkān) are inseparable from contingent essences.
4
Understand necessary of mental existence
These are secondary intelligibles like quiddity (māhiyya) that are inseparable only when considered in the mind.
5
Appreciate the author's closing remark
Perpetuity always involves a causal necessity; otherwise it would not exist.

The fifth universal: the common accident, and it is the universal that is predicated on different realities, like 'walking' in relation to man. The Existence of the Necessary Being: Identity with Essence
Hashia argument against external attribute of existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the hashia's conditional reductio argument against the philosophers' view that the existence of the Necessary Being is an external attribute.

Core Idea
The hashia refutes the philosophers by showing that an external attribute would necessarily be an effect, leading to contradiction.

In this passage from a hashia (super-commentary) on the five universals, the author presents a precise logical argument against the philosophers' claim that existence is identical to the essence of the Necessary Being. The hashia shows that if existence were an external attribute, it would be an effect—contradicting the necessity of the Necessary Being. By understanding this argument, you will grasp how conditional reasoning is used to refine theological positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Locate the statement of the philosophers in the Arabic. What exactly is their position? They argue that the existence of the Necessary Being is identical to His essence, otherwise it would be an external attribute.
The philosophers reject external attributes due to composition.
2
Extract the conditional premise
The hashia states: if existence were an external attribute (عارض), then it would be an effect (معلول). Find the 'وإلا' clause and understand the consequence.
This is the first conditional link.
3
Analyze the disjunctive consequence
The effect must be either self-caused or caused by another. The hashia splits into two possibilities: 'فإما لنفسه وإما لغيره'.
This is an exhaustive disjunction.
4
Examine each branch for absurdity
Self-causation would entail priority over the essence (existent before existence), other-causation would make the Necessary Being dependent. Both lead to contradictions.
This is a reductio ad absurdum (khalaf).
5
Conclude the refutation
Since both branches are impossible, the original assumption (existence as external) is false. Therefore existence must be identical to essence.
This confirms the theologians' position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكليات الخمس كوجود الواجب على مذهب المتكلمين)، قال في «الحاشية»: اعلم: أن الحكماء قالوا: إن وجود الواجب عينه وإلا لكان خارجا عارضا له؛ لامتناع التركيب، فيكون معلولا؛ إذ كل خارج معلول بالضرورة، فإما لنفسه، فيلزم تقدمه بالوجود عليه فيكون قبل الوجود موجودا، وإما لغيره، فيكون الواجب محتاجا في وجوده إلى الغير، هذا خلف .
Chapter on the Five Universals: like the existence of the Necessary Being according to the school of the theologians. He said in the Hashia: Know that the philosophers said: The existence of the Necessary Being is His very essence, otherwise it would be an external accident to Him, because composition is impossible; then it would be an effect, for everything external is necessarily an effect. So either it is [an effect] of itself, which would entail its priority in existence over Him, so He would be existent before existence; or it is of another, then the Necessary Being would be in need of another for His existence. This is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is a critical note from a hashia (super-commentary) on a discussion of the five universals (al-kulliyat al-khams). It uses the example of the existence of the Necessary Being (Wajib al-Wujud) to illustrate a logical argument about identity and attributes. The author presents the position of the theologians (mutakallimun) who hold that the existence of the Necessary Being is identical to His essence (dhāt). In contrast, the philosophers (hukama) are said to argue that if existence were an external attribute (āriḍ), it would be an accident, leading to composition in the divine essence—which is impossible. Consequently, that attribute would be an effect (ma'lul). The argument proceeds: if it is an effect of itself, then it would have priority over the essence, meaning the essence would be existent before existence, which is absurd. If it is an effect of another, then the Necessary Being would be dependent on that other for its existence, which contradicts its necessity. Thus, the philosophers' view leads to a contradiction (khalaf). The hashia here refines the understanding by clarifying the logical structure of the philosophers' argument and showing its inconsistency. The lesson is that in discussing universals, one must be careful about the identity of essential attributes and the nature of necessary existence.

Key Terms

The Five Universals
beginner
5 min
Three Types of Universal: Logical, Natural, Mental
Defines the three types of universal: logical, natural, mental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the logical, natural, and mental universal and correctly classify any given universal term.

Core Idea
Every universal has three aspects: concept, referent, and their combination.

In logic, universals like 'human' or 'animal' are not simple. They have three distinct aspects: the concept in the mind (logical), the external reality it refers to (natural), and the combination of both (mental). Understanding this trichotomy is essential for precise discussions in Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concept (logical universal)
The logical universal is the meaning or form itself, e.g., the concept 'human' as a mental content.
This is what the logician studies.
2
Identify the subject (natural universal)
The natural universal is the external reality that the concept refers to, e.g., individual humans in the world.
It is the 'carrier' of the concept.
3
Identify the combination (mental universal)
The mental universal is the integrated notion of both the concept and its external instantiation considered together.
It is the compound of logical and natural.
4
Apply to the five universals
The same trichotomy applies to each of the five universals (genus, species, etc.) and even to particulars.
E.g., logical genus is the concept, natural genus is the external referent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة لبحث الكلي: مَنْهُومُ الْكُلِّيِّ الذي مر يُسَمَّى كُلِّيًّا مَنْطِقِيًّا، وَمَعْرُوضُ ذَلِكَ الْمَفْهُومِ يُسَمَّى كُلِّيًّا طَبَعِيًّا، وَالْمَجْمُوعُ مِنَ الْعَارِضِ وَالْمَعْرُوضِ يُسَمَّى كُلِّيًّا عَقْلِيًّا. وَكَذَا الْكُلِّيَاتُ الْخَمْسُ، بَلِ الْجُزْئِيُّ أَيْضًا، مِنْهَا مَنْطِقِيٌّ وَطَبَعِيٌّ وَعَقْلِيٌّ. فَمَفْهُومُ الْجِنْسِ جِنْسٌ مَنْطِقِيٌّ وَمَعْرُوضُهُ جِنْسٌ طَبِيعِيٌّ، وَالْمَجْمُوعُ جِنْسٌ عَقْلِيٌّ، وَهَكَذَا. ثُمَّ الطَّبَعِيُّ لَهُ اعْتِبَارَاتٌ ثَلَاثَةٌ: بِشَرْطِ لَا شَيْءٍ، أَيْ بِشَرْطِ عَدَمِ التَّعَيُّنِ وَعَدَمِ سَائِرِ الْعَوَارِضِ، يُسَمَّى مُجَرَّدَةً؛ وَبِشَرْطِ شَيْءٍ، هُوَ التَّعَيُّنُ وَغَيْرُهُ مِنَ الْعَوَارِضِ، يُسَمَّى مَخْلُوطَةً؛ وَلَا بِشَرْطِ شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْعَوَارِضِ وَعَدَمِهَا، يُسَمَّى مُطْلَقَةً. وَهِيَ مِنْ حَيْثُ هِيَ، مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنْ جَمِيعِ الْعَوَارِضِ وَاللَّوَاحِقِ الْخَارِجِيَّةِ، لَيْسَتْ مَوْجُودَةً وَلَا مَعْدُومَةً وَلَا شَيْئًا مِنَ الْعَوَارِضِ، مِنَ الْوَحْدَةِ وَالْكُثْرَةِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ في نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ لَا يَخْلُو عَنْهَا.
Conclusion of the discussion on the universal: The concept of the universal mentioned earlier is called logical universal; the subject of that concept is called natural universal; and the combination of the attribute and the subject is called mental universal. And likewise the five universals, and even the particular, among them are logical, natural, and mental. So the concept of genus is logical genus, its subject is natural genus, and the combination is mental genus, and so on. Then the natural has three considerations: with the condition of nothing, i.e. with the condition of the absence of determination and absence of all other accidents, it is called abstract; with the condition of something, which is determination and other accidents, it is called mixed; and without any condition of accidents or their absence, it is called absolute. And in itself, disregarding all external accidents and concomitants, it is not existent nor non-existent nor any of the accidents, such as unity and multiplicity, even though in actual fact it is not free from them.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage defines the threefold classification of every universal (and even particulars) in logic: logical, natural, and mental. The 'logical universal' is the concept itself as it exists in the mind, e.g., the concept 'human'. The 'natural universal' is the external reality that the concept refers to, e.g., individual humans in the world. The 'mental universal' is the combination of the concept and its external instantiation considered together. This applies to all five universals (genus, species, differentia, property, accident) and even to particular concepts. The author then focuses on the natural universal and discusses three ways it can be considered: (1) abstract (mujarrada) – considered without any accidents or determination, e.g., 'human' considered as a bare essence; (2) mixed (makhluṭa) – considered with specific accidents, e.g., 'human' as tall or short; (3) absolute (muṭlaqa) – considered without any condition, i.e., neither with nor without accidents. The absolute universal in itself is free from existence, non-existence, and all accidents, even though in reality it never exists without them. This distinction is crucial in Kalam and Fiqh discussions about the existence of universals, e.g., whether the universal 'human' exists in the external world or only in the mind.

Key Terms
كلي منطقي (kulli mantiqi): The concept of a universal as it exists in the mind; the form or meaning considered logically. [Note: No significant shift; consistent across texts.]
كلي طبيعي (kulli tabi'i): The external reality that instantiates the universal concept; the subject of the logical universal. [Note: Some disagreement about whether it exists externally or only mentally; here it is treated as the external referent.]
كلي عقلي (kulli 'aqli): The combination of the logical and natural universals; the concept plus its external instantiation. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, the combination of the concept and its subject is called:
A) Logical universal
B) Natural universal
C) Mental universal
D) Abstract universal

Key Takeaway
Every universal and even particular concepts have a threefold division: logical (concept), natural (external instantiation), and mental (combination).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the universal 'animal' and identify its logical, natural, and mental aspects. Write one sentence for each.

Three Considerations of the Natural Universal
Explains the three considerations of the natural universal: mujarrada, makhluṭa, muṭlaqa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three considerations of the natural universal: abstract, mixed, and absolute.

Core Idea
The natural universal can be considered without accidents (abstract), with accidents (mixed), or unconditioned (absolute).

The natural universal, the external reality, can be looked at in three ways: without any accidents (mujarrada), with specific accidents (makhluṭa), or without any condition about accidents (muṭlaqa). This distinction is vital for understanding how universals relate to individuals in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'with the condition of nothing'
This means considering the essence stripped of all determinations and accidents, e.g., 'human' as a pure nature.
This is called mujarrada (abstract).
2
Understand 'with the condition of something'
This means considering the essence with specific accidents, e.g., 'human' as tall or short.
This is called makhluṭa (mixed).
3
Understand 'without any condition'
This means considering the essence in itself, not requiring or excluding any accidents.
This is called muṭlaqa (absolute).
4
Note the absolute's neutrality
The absolute universal in itself is not existent nor non-existent nor any accident, even though in reality it never exists without them.
This shows the universal's independence from external attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة لبحث الكلي: مَنْهُومُ الْكُلِّيِّ الذي مر يُسَمَّى كُلِّيًّا مَنْطِقِيًّا، وَمَعْرُوضُ ذَلِكَ الْمَفْهُومِ يُسَمَّى كُلِّيًّا طَبَعِيًّا، وَالْمَجْمُوعُ مِنَ الْعَارِضِ وَالْمَعْرُوضِ يُسَمَّى كُلِّيًّا عَقْلِيًّا. وَكَذَا الْكُلِّيَاتُ الْخَمْسُ، بَلِ الْجُزْئِيُّ أَيْضًا، مِنْهَا مَنْطِقِيٌّ وَطَبَعِيٌّ وَعَقْلِيٌّ. فَمَفْهُومُ الْجِنْسِ جِنْسٌ مَنْطِقِيٌّ وَمَعْرُوضُهُ جِنْسٌ طَبِيعِيٌّ، وَالْمَجْمُوعُ جِنْسٌ عَقْلِيٌّ، وَهَكَذَا. ثُمَّ الطَّبَعِيُّ لَهُ اعْتِبَارَاتٌ ثَلَاثَةٌ: بِشَرْطِ لَا شَيْءٍ، أَيْ بِشَرْطِ عَدَمِ التَّعَيُّنِ وَعَدَمِ سَائِرِ الْعَوَارِضِ، يُسَمَّى مُجَرَّدَةً؛ وَبِشَرْطِ شَيْءٍ، هُوَ التَّعَيُّنُ وَغَيْرُهُ مِنَ الْعَوَارِضِ، يُسَمَّى مَخْلُوطَةً؛ وَلَا بِشَرْطِ شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْعَوَارِضِ وَعَدَمِهَا، يُسَمَّى مُطْلَقَةً. وَهِيَ مِنْ حَيْثُ هِيَ، مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنْ جَمِيعِ الْعَوَارِضِ وَاللَّوَاحِقِ الْخَارِجِيَّةِ، لَيْسَتْ مَوْجُودَةً وَلَا مَعْدُومَةً وَلَا شَيْئًا مِنَ الْعَوَارِضِ، مِنَ الْوَحْدَةِ وَالْكُثْرَةِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ في نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ لَا يَخْلُو عَنْهَا.
Conclusion of the discussion on the universal: The concept of the universal mentioned earlier is called logical universal; the subject of that concept is called natural universal; and the combination of the attribute and the subject is called mental universal. And likewise the five universals, and even the particular, among them are logical, natural, and mental. So the concept of genus is logical genus, its subject is natural genus, and the combination is mental genus, and so on. Then the natural has three considerations: with the condition of nothing, i.e. with the condition of the absence of determination and absence of all other accidents, it is called abstract; with the condition of something, which is determination and other accidents, it is called mixed; and without any condition of accidents or their absence, it is called absolute. And in itself, disregarding all external accidents and concomitants, it is not existent nor non-existent nor any of the accidents, such as unity and multiplicity, even though in actual fact it is not free from them.

Commentary
This passage defines the threefold classification of every universal (and even particulars) in logic: logical, natural, and mental. The 'logical universal' is the concept itself as it exists in the mind, e.g., the concept 'human'. The 'natural universal' is the external reality that the concept refers to, e.g., individual humans in the world. The 'mental universal' is the combination of the concept and its external instantiation considered together. This applies to all five universals (genus, species, differentia, property, accident) and even to particular concepts. The author then focuses on the natural universal and discusses three ways it can be considered: (1) abstract (mujarrada) – considered without any accidents or determination, e.g., 'human' considered as a bare essence; (2) mixed (makhluṭa) – considered with specific accidents, e.g., 'human' as tall or short; (3) absolute (muṭlaqa) – considered without any condition, i.e., neither with nor without accidents. The absolute universal in itself is free from existence, non-existence, and all accidents, even though in reality it never exists without them. This distinction is crucial in Kalam and Fiqh discussions about the existence of universals, e.g., whether the universal 'human' exists in the external world or only in the mind.

Key Terms
مجرّدة (mujarrada): The natural universal considered without any accidents or determination; a pure essence. [Note: In other contexts, mujarrad may mean 'immaterial'; here it means 'abstracted from accidents'.]
مخلوطة (makhluṭa): The natural universal considered with specific accidents; the instantiated individual. [Note: Stable meaning.]
مطلقة (muṭlaqa): The natural universal considered without any condition of having or lacking accidents; considered in itself. [Note: This term varies; in logic it often means 'absolute' as opposed to 'restricted'.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The natural universal has three modes: abstract (free of accidents), mixed (with accidents), and absolute (unconditioned).

Three Aspects of Every Universal: Logical, Natural, Rational
Author defines the tripartite division of the universal concept and its application to the five universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the logical, natural, and rational aspects of any universal concept and apply this distinction to each of the five universals.

Core Idea
Every universal concept has three faces: mental, real, and composite.

In this passage, the author introduces a crucial tripartite distinction that applies to every universal term—whether genus, species, or any of the five universals. Understanding this distinction will sharpen your ability to analyse definitions and avoid confusion between concepts and their instances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three elements
Look at the Arabic: مَفْهُوم (concept), مَعْرُوض (subject of the concept), and الْمَجْمُوع (composite). These correspond to logical, natural, and rational respectively.
2
Define each aspect
The logical universal is the mental concept (e.g., the idea of 'animal'). The natural universal is the external object that the concept characterizes (e.g., actual animals). The rational universal is the combination of both in the mind.
3
Apply to genus
The example given: the concept of genus is the logical genus; the actual things that are genera are the natural genus; the mental composite is the rational genus.
4
Extend to all five universals and particulars
The author says it applies to all five universals and even to particular concepts. Verify with another universal like species.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(خاتمة) لبحث الكلي (مَفْهُومُ الْكُلِّي) الذي مر (يُسَمَّى كُلِّيًّا مَنْطِقِيًّا، وَمَعْرُوضُ ذَلِكَ الْمَفْهُوم) أي الذي يعرضه ذلك المفهوم بعد حصوله في العقل (يُسَمَّى كُلِّيًّا طَبَعِيًّا، وَالْمَجْمُوعُ مِنَ الْعَارِضِ وَالْمَعْرُوض يُسَمَّى كُلِّيًّا عَقْلِيًّا، وَكَذَا الْكُلِّيَاتُ الْخَمْسُ)، بل الجزئي أيضا، (مِنْهَا مَنْطِقِيٌّ وَطَبَعِيٌّ وَعَقْلِيٌّ)، فَمَفْهُومُ الْجِنْسِ جِنْسٌ مَنْطِقِيٌّ وَمَعْرُوضُهُ جِنْسٌ طَبِيعِيٌّ، وَالْمَجْمُوعُ جِنْسٌ عَقْلِيٌّ، وَهَكَذَا.
(Conclusion) of the investigation of the universal: the concept of the universal that has passed is called a logical universal; the subject of that concept—i.e., that which is characterized by that concept after its occurrence in the mind—is called a natural universal; and the composite of the attribute and the subject is called a rational universal. And likewise the five universals—indeed the particular also—have from them logical, natural, and rational. So the concept of genus is a logical genus, its subject is a natural genus, and the composite is a rational genus, and so on.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage defines the threefold division of any universal concept (such as genus, species, difference, etc.). The author explains that when we speak of 'universal,' we can consider three aspects: first, the mental concept itself (e.g., the idea of 'animal')—this is called the logical universal (kulli mantiqi) because it is the term or concept used in logic; second, the external object or instance that the concept applies to (e.g., actual animals in the world)—this is the natural universal (kulli tabi'i) because it exists in nature; third, the combination of the concept and its object in the mind—this is the rational universal (kulli 'aqli) because it is a mental composite. The author then applies this to the five universals (genus, species, difference, property, accident) and even to particular concepts, all of which can be viewed in these three respects. For example, the concept 'genus' itself is a logical genus, the actual things that are genera (like 'animal' as a category) are natural genera, and the mental construct 'genus as such' is a rational genus.

Key Terms
كلي منطقي (kulli mantiqi): The concept (mafhum) of a universal considered as a mental attribute or logical term, e.g., the idea of 'animal'. [Note: This division is standard in post-Avicennan logic; earlier logicians (e.g., Aristotle) did not distinguish these three explicitly.]
كلي طبيعي (kulli tabi'i): The external object (ma'rud) that the universal concept characterizes, e.g., actual animals in the world.
كلي عقلي (kulli 'aqli): The composite of the logical concept and its external object as unified in the mind.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If we consider the concept of 'human' (insan) as a mental idea, which type of universal is it?
A) Logical universal
B) Natural universal
C) Rational universal
D) Particular

Key Takeaway
The tripartite division of the universal into logical, natural, and rational applies to every universal term and is essential for precise logical analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Paradox of Suspended Opposites at the Essence Level
Explains the suspension of opposites at the essence level.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between restricted affirmation, restricted negation, and the negation of a restricted term, and explain why at the level of essence alone both restricted opposites can be false without violating the law of non-contradiction.

Core Idea
Restricted opposites are not true contradictories; both can be false at the level of essence.

In the discussion of the five universals, logicians examine the quiddity (mahiyya) at various levels of abstraction. At the level where only the essence and its essentials are considered, a curious paradox arises: if we ask whether the quiddity in itself is existent or not existent, both answers may seem false. This passage unpacks the logical distinction between restricted negation and negation of the restricted, showing that the apparent suspension of opposites is only superficial.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the level of essence
The 'level of essence' (martabat al-dhat) considers only the quiddity itself and its essential attributes. At this level, no accidental or external predication applies.
This is a key concept in the five universals discussion.
2
Identify the two forms of questioning
The author gives two forms: (1) 'Is the quiddity in itself existent or not in itself existent?' (negation before restriction) and (2) 'Is the quiddity in itself existent or is it not existent?' (negation after restriction).
The placement of the negation relative to the restriction is critical.
3
Distinguish between restricted negation and negation-of-the-restricted
In form (1), the negation modifies the restriction ('not in itself'), making it a restricted negation. In form (2), the negation falls on the whole restricted affirmation ('not [existent in itself]'), which is the true contradictory of the restricted affirmation.
This distinction resolves the paradox.
4
Apply the law of non-contradiction
True contradictories (affirmation and negation of the same thing under the same condition) cannot both be false. But the two restricted propositions (restricted affirmation and restricted negation) are not contradictories; their real contradictory is the negation of the restricted affirmation.
Thus the law is preserved; the apparent suspension is explained.

فصل في الكليات الخمس. بعضها. والسر فيه أن في هذه المرتبة ذاتها وذاتياتها، ومصداق حمل شيء في هذه المرتبة هو نفس الذات، فلا يحمل في هذه المرتبة إلا الذات والذاتيات . فإذا سئل عن هذه المرتبة بطرفي النقيض بأن يقال: الماهية في حد ذاتها موجودة مثلا، أو ليست في حد ذاتها موجودة بأن يقدم السلب على القيد، فالجواب السلب وإن أخر السلب عن القيد، ويقال : الماهية في ذاتها موجودة أو ليست بموجودة كان الجواب لا هذا ولا ذاك ، بل لا جواب أصلا، إلا أن يؤخر في اللفظ ويقدم في المعنى . والسر فيه أن السلب وارد في الصورة الأولى على المقيد، فيكون نقيضا للثبوت المقيد ، فالسؤال عن أحد النقيضين، فإذا كذب الإيجاب صدق السلب بخلاف الثانية ؛ لأن السلب فيها مقيد كالإيجاب، والمقيدان لا يتناقضان. : والحاصل أن في تلك المرتبة ليس إلا الذات والذاتيات، فلا جرم كذب المقيدان؛ إذ حاصلهما أن الثبوت واقع له أو سلبه. وبالجملة: أن الإيجابات كلها كاذبة عدولية كانت أو تحصيلية والسلوب الواردة على الإيجابات المقيدة كلها صوادق. وما قيل السلوب البسيطة الواردة في المراتب الوترية صوادق، وأما الواقعة في المراتب الشفعية كواذب؛ بناء على مساوقتها الإيجاب، فيرده ما يحققه المصنف من أن السلب لا تضاف إلى السلب. ففي هذه المرتبة ارتفع النقيضان، هكذا وقع في عبارة غير واحد من المحققين. والحق أن نقيض الثبوت المقيد سلب المقيد لا السلب المقيد فالمرتفعان - وهما الثبوت والسلب المقيدان - ليسا نقيضين، وما هو نقيض الثبوت المقيد هو سلب المقيد ليس مرتفعا كيف وارتفاع النقيضين محال في كل مرتبة بالضرورة الأولية ألا ترى أن الحكم بارتفاع الثبوت حكم بصدق الرفع الذي هو النقيض، والحكم بارتفاع السلب مساوق للحكم بصدق الإيجاب، فاجتمع النقيضان. فقد ظهر أن عباراتهم مؤولة بأن ما يرى في بادئ الرأي نقيضين أو النقيضين التصوريين مرتفعان، والله أعلم بمراد عباده.
Chapter on the five universals. Part of them. And the secret in it is that in this level (are) the essence and its essentials, and the truth-referent of the predication of something in this level is the essence itself, so nothing is predicated in this level except the essence and its essentials. So if one asks about this level with the two extremes of contradiction by saying: 'the quiddity in itself is existent, for example, or it is not in itself existent' by placing the negation before the restriction, so the answer is the negation, even if one delays the negation after the restriction, and one says: 'the quiddity in itself is existent or it is not existent', the answer would be 'neither this nor that', nay there is no answer at all, unless one delays it in wording and advances it in meaning. And the secret in it is that the negation in the first form falls on the restricted, so it is a contradictory to the restricted affirmation, so the question is about one of the two contradictories. So when the affirmation is false, the negation is true, contrary to the second; because the negation in it is restricted like the affirmation, and the two restricted ones are not contradictories. And the conclusion is that in that level there is nothing but the essence and its essentials, so necessarily the two restricted ones are false, since their product is that the affirmation occurs for it or its negation. And in summary: all affirmations are false, whether they are privative or positive, and the negations falling on the restricted affirmations are all true. And what is said: the simple negations occurring in the odd levels are true, while those occurring in the even levels are false, based on their being equivalent to affirmation, is refuted by what the author establishes: that the negation is not added to the negation. So in this level the two opposites are raised. Thus it occurs in the expression of more than one of the investigators. And the truth is that the contradictory of the restricted affirmation is the negation of the restricted, not the restricted negation. So the two raised ones—the restricted affirmation and the restricted negation—are not opposites, and that which is the contradictory of the restricted affirmation is the negation of the restricted, which is not raised. How can it be? For the raising of the two opposites is impossible in every level by primary necessity. Do you not see that the judgment of the raising of the affirmation is a judgment of the truth of the raising that is the negation? And the judgment of the raising of the negation is equivalent to the judgment of the truth of affirmation, so the two opposites would concur. It has become clear that their expressions are interpreted as meaning that what appears at first sight to be two opposites, or the two conceptual opposites, are raised. And God knows best the intent of His servants.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on a point within the chapter on the five universals. The author explains a subtle logical issue regarding the level (martaba) where only the essence (dhat) and its essentials (dhatiyyat) are considered. At this level, predication is only of the essence itself. The author then explores how questions of existence and non-existence apply to this level. He distinguishes two forms of questioning: one where the negation precedes the restriction (i.e., 'it is not in itself existent') and one where negation follows the restriction ('it is or is not existent'). In the first form, the negation is of the restricted, so it contradicts the restricted affirmation, and one is true. In the second form, both the affirmation and negation are restricted, so they are not contradictories and both can be false. The author concludes that at this level, all affirmations are false and all negations of restricted affirmations are true. He then addresses a claim about simple negations in odd vs. even levels, refuting it with the principle that negation does not attach to negation. Finally, he explains the apparent paradox of the 'raising of the two opposites' (irtifa' al-naqidayn), clarifying that what are raised are not true opposites but conceptual opposites, and that in reality, the contradictory of the restricted affirmation is the negation of the restricted, which remains true. So the claim that both opposites are suspended is interpreted metaphorically.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, at the level of the essence alone, what is the status of the restricted affirmation and restricted negation?
A) Both are true.
B) Both are false.
C) One is true, one is false.
D) They are not opposites.

Key Takeaway
At the level of essence, the restricted affirmation and restricted negation are both false because they are not true contradictories; only the negation of the restricted affirmation is the genuine contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition 'The world is in itself eternal.' Is this a restricted affirmation? What would its true contradictory be? Apply the lesson's distinction to determine whether both can be false at the level of essence.
?
In usul al-fiqh, an act is often defined as 'obligatory in itself' (wajib bi-dhatihi). Using the lesson's framework, explain why the restricted negation 'not obligatory in itself' is not the true contradictory of the restricted affirmation, and what would be the correct contradictory.
